Flarkets
6.03      Agriculture.  The incremental production generated by the project
ujuld have ready markets. Increased wheat and feed grain production is need-
ei domestically to sustain self-sufficiency for these products, thus alleviat-
ilag severe supply problems resulting from rapid population and income growth
aad from the shortage of land that can be allocated to grain production.
BEef and egg production and the majority of the increased citrus and early
vagetables production would be consumed locally on highly absorptive markets;
arout 25%o of citrus and early vegetables would be exported, mainly to Western
E-irope.
6*04      Agroindustries.  The major share of citrus and grape concentrate
aid about 50% of the incremental tomato paste and olive oil production are
ecpected to be exported to Western Europe. The balance could be easily
a'sorbcd on the domestic markets which are characterized by high income
eLasticity of demand (0.6 for vegetables and 0.8 for fruit). As a result
o. investments in cold-stores and packing houses, part of the stored and
picked apples would be exported to Iran and other mid-east countries where
dimand for apples has increased markedly over recent years.  Marketing of
p'ecooled fruits (such as peaches, apricots, cherries) to Europe would
b- enhanced through transport facilities (refrigerated trailers and ferry
b:ats) financed under this and the Fresh Fruit and Vegetable Export Project.
F!eds would be used in Turkey's rapidly growing livestock industry, and
tle proposed slaughterhouses are urgently required to replace old, unsanitary
aid operationally inefficient plants and to increase cold storage of existing
s'aughter plants.



- 30 -
6.05      Ferryship.  The proposed ferryship would increase the annual
transport capacity by about 130,000 tons, thus alleviating a severe transport
bottleneck. The major benefits would result from saved transport costs on
122,500 tons and increased fruit and vegetable exports (7,500 tons). Further-
more, the proposed ferryship would reduce spoilage and increase exDort prices
of fruits and vegetables through more timely and reliable delivery.
Prices
6.06      Farmgate prices for most of the relevant commodities are set through
minimum price support programs, the major exceptions being for livestock,
fruits and vegetables (see Annex 1, paras 13-17). Both output and input
prices during appraisal (May 1975) are more or less in line with world market
prices. Because of its limited scope, the project's impact on both domestic
and international prices would be negligible with the exception of beef prices,
where the project's incremental production would cause domestic beef prices to
increase at a slightly slower rate than that at which international beef prices
are expected to rise. The proposed investments in fruit and vegetable storing,
cooling, canning and processing would induce a slight increase in the respec-
tive farm prices resulting in additional farm income.
B. Financial Benefits
6.07      Some 20,000 farming families representing about 120,000 persons are
,expected to benefit from the project, most of whom would be farming under un-
favorable conditions in Central and Eastern Turkey with a fairly high potential
for expanding grain and beef production. The financial benefits generated
through the investments in the supervised credit and beef fattening compo-
nents are satisfactory (Annex 3, Tables 6-12, Annex 5, Table 2). Net incre-
mental income and financial rate of return (17-71%) would be sufficiently high
to make the proposed investments attractive. M4ay 1975 farmgate prices have
been applied in calculating the returns; any inflationary pressure would be
expected to affect both costs and benefits to the same extent, thus maintain-
ing the present relationship between them. Sensitivity tests assuming higher
costs or lower production showed satisfactory results in all cases.
6.08      Investments in agroindustries, mainly in the Mlediterranean and
Aegian regions, and in the ferryship would yield satisfactory rates of return
(16-27%) and attractive cash flows (Annexes 4 and 6). Sensitivity tests were
satisfactory for increases in investment costs. However, the agroindustry
investments proved to be extremely sensitive to increases in operating costs
or decreases in sales, which is typical for investments where the costs of
raw material represent a high portion of the total production costs.



- 31 -
C. Economic Benefits
Foreign Exchange Earnings
6.09      The bulk of project investments would generate outputs for local
consumption, mainly beef, eggs, wheat and barley. Most of the direct export
earnings would come from the agroindustries component, mainly from fruit and
vegetable storage and processing. At full production the project would gen-
erate export earnings (in 1975 prices):
TL 14 million        (US$1 million )      from agriculture
TL 762 million       (US$54 million)      from agroindustries
Additional foreign exchange earnings would result from the ferryboat invest-
ments providing transport capacity for both agricultural and non-agricultural
products. Taking into account (i) direct exports (TL 776 million); (ii)
indirect exports resulting from the ferryship (TL 60 million); and (iii)
foreign exchange savings by import substitution of grain production (TL 68
million), the total foreign exchange earnings would amount to TL 900 million
(US$65 million) annually. This would be particularly important to Turkey
in view of its rapid decrease of foreign exchange reserves which occured
since 1974.
Employment
6.10      The project would generate substantial employment opportunities
both directly within the firms receiving credit and indirectly by increased
demand for inputs to be used in the project.

Employment would be generated throughout the country with agricultural employ-
ment opportunities being created predominantlv in the less developed regions
of Central and East Anatolia, where disguised unemployment is relatively high.
The proposed agroindustry and ferryship investments would result in additional
urban employment primarily in the developed western parts of Turkey.  In view
of deteriorating prospects for labor migration to western Europe, the project's
employment effect would be important.



- 32 -
Target Group and Income Benefits
6.11      Turkey's average 1975 per-capita income is TL 8,400 (US$600).  The
average without-project per-capita income of farmers' families receiving credit
under the supervised credit program would be TL 3,890 (US$278). Project in-
vestments would generate an increase of average per-capita income to TL 4,960
(US$354) before and TL 4,440 (US$317) after debt service. Within the beef
fattening component, the average per capita income of the benefiting farmers
would increase from TL 3,700 (US$264) to TL 5,520 (US$394) before and to
TL 5,250 (US$375) after debt services.
6.12      Principal beneficiaries of agroindustries investments would be those
entrepreneurs who may already have relatively high incomes, except in the
cases of cooperatives with memberships of medium and small scale farmers.
However, the income impact would also reach the primary producers who are in
many cases small and medium scale farmers with product contract arrangements
with processprs. For the ferryboat and slaughterhouses, SEE's would be the
subborrowers, resulting in positive effects on public income.
6.13      About 88% of the farmers benefitting directly from the agricultural
components would receive 74% of the loan amount; these farmers would belong
to the "target group" (US$285 per-capita as of 1975). Additional incomes
would accrue:
-    to workers directly or indirectlv employed under the project
(para 6.10); and
-    to consumers benefiting from the increased supply of beef and
other commodities.
Resource Allocation
6.14      The strengthening of the institutions involved, mainly TCZB and PU
would result in substantial benefits from improved resource allocation at the
investment design level. The staff of TCZB and PU trained under this project
would also be responsible for operations beyond the project. Their experience
could thus result in further resource savings.
Economic Rates of Return
6.15      The economy is expected to realize a return of 36% from total proj-
ect investments, including physical contingencies. The individual components
are estimated to yield the following results:

The costs of all supporting services have been included, however the resource

shadow priced according to skill, location and season. For all domestic
inputs (including labor) and for outputs resulting in in-creased consumption,
shadow exchange rates have been used. A sensitivity test was applied to test
the project against changes in project costs and production results. By
increasing the project costs by 10% the rate of return would drop to 19%.
By decreasing the value of the benefits by 10% the overall return would
fail to 18% (Annex 11).

and

(i) submit to the Bank for its prior review subloan appraisals for

5.08); (b) the first sub-loan of each type for agroindustry
investments (para 5.08); (c) the first subloan of each type
of on-farm investment (para 5.09);
(ii)  create a special revolving fund for sub-loan repayments received
through TSF and enable TSF to reuse resources from this account

(iii)  set aside half of the SCP medium-and long-term loan amount for

for a Bank loan of $63 million equivalent for a term of 15 years inclutding

APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT AND AGROINDUSTRIES PROJECT
TURKEY
The Agricultural Sector
A. General Information
Introduction
1.        Agriculture remains an important sector in the Turkish economy
comprising in 1974, 26% of GDP, contributing about 60% of the foreign ex-
change earnings and much of the industrial raw materials, and employing 63%
of the work force. Agricultural growth, which averaged about 4% p.a. (com-
pound) between 1969 and 1974, has been relatively stable. It compares to the
average global agricultural growth rate of 7.4% p.a. for the same period and
has outpaced the population growth rate of 2.5% p.a. The Third Five-Year
Plan (1973-77) anticipates a 4.5% growth rate for the agricultural sector with
the larger increase in livestock (5%), compared to crop production (4%). The
development achieved so far has been disparate between types of commodities,
industrial crops recording the highest rate and cereals the lowest. Live-
stock production growth has been poor compared to that of crops, despite the
demand increase for livestock products. Regional growth has varied, e.g.
those regions with a predominance of cereal crops (Anatolian Plateau) and
livestock (eastern Turkey) have fared poorly compared to the other more
fertile areas.
Agricultural Resources 1/
2.        Turkey has a total area of 78 million ha of which 54 million ha
are used for agriculture. The cultivated area amounts to 25 million ha, of
which 9 million ha are fallow. From 1961 to 1973, the total area cultivated
increased by 8% (1.9 million ha), half of which was sown and half kept fallow.
This expansion of cultivated land caused a decrease in meadows and pasture.
However, conversion from pasture to cultivated land cannot continue at this
rate since virtually all arable land is now being cultivated. Major crops
are wheat (8.5 million ha), barley (2.6 million ha), cotton (0.7 million ha)
and sunflower (0.4 million ha). An area of about 2 million ha is irrigated,
which is substantially less than the area which could be irrigated economically.
1/   Refer to Tables 1-5.



ANNEX 1
Page 2
The high proportion of fallow and the relatively low crop yields are due mainly
to the shortage of water, but also to inefficient water use on irrigated land.
Turkey has large livestock resources (13 million cattle, 40 million sheep and
119 million goats) which are concentrated mainly in the eastern and central
parts of the country.
RFeional Production Patterns
3.        Climatic and soil conditions vary widely, resulting in five major
regions with substantially different agricultural production patterns. The
Central Anatolian Plateau is the largest of these regions. About 800-1,000 m
above  sea level, it is characterized by hot summers, cold winters and 250-400
nIm annual precipitation, and it is here that most of Turkey's fallow land is
situated. Dry farming, with wheat as the main crop, and a cereal/fallow
rotation is predominant, because of weather conditions, central Anatolian
agriculture experiences heavy fluctuations in wheat yields. Eastern Anatolia,
a mountainous area with extremely cold winters. is primarily used for live-
stock production. However, both eastern and central Turkey have several
fertile vallevs with irrigated agriculture where sugarbeet, sunflower, apples,
peaches and apricots are grown. By contrast, the Mediterranean region with
centers in the Adana-Mersin and the Antalya plains has mild winters and here
irrigated cultivation of citrus, cotton and early vegetable prevails. Pro-
ductivity per hectare and farm incomes are higher than in the other regions.
The Marmara and Aegian reeion is rather heterozeneous. Generallv. it is not
as mild as the Mediterranean region, although in some areas citrus and cotton
production is possible. Other dominant crops are olive and fruit trees, grapes,
tobacco, vegetables, sunflower and sugarbeets. Mild and humid conditions pre-
vail in the Black Sea region where hazelnuts and tea are the most important
crops.
Land Ownersh
4.        The land holding situation in Turkey is not well documented because
title to much of the land has not been registered and cadastral survey is
proceeding slowly. According to the 1965 census, the average size of holding
is 6.9 ha. More detailed information about farm size distribution is given
below:

Most of the land is being farmed in small units, although the 1970 census shows
some extremes in the distribution of land ranging from 4,000 units of 1,000 ha
cr more to 800,000 units of less than 1 ha. Most of the large farms are sit--
uated in the southeast.
5.        Information on tenancy systems is virtually nonexistent.  The 1950
census revealed the following status of farm units classified according to
the land tenure svstem:
Farm Units
Landowners                                      72.6

Tenant Farmers                                   0.6
Sharecroppers                                    2.5
Others                                           2.R
TOTAL                                     100.0
Although this distribution may be changing towards owner/tenant or owner/
sharecropper farms, most farmers in Turkey own all or the major portion of
their land. Tenancy and sharecropping arrangements vary with the crops grown
End land quality. Tenancy contracts for grain are usually for one year, for
cther crops up Lo 5 years.  In 11970, che average cash rent for wheat land was
about TL 400/ha. The 1972 cash rents for irrigated cotton land in Adana and
Denizli provinces varied from TL 1500-3000/ha 1/. In grain production,
tenancy arrangements are less common than sharecropping, where the owner pro-
'.-ides land and seed and receives 50% of the output. Observations have con-
firmed that such contracts put the tenant in a rather unfavorable position
and provide few incentives to improve farming techniques. Combined owner/
sharecropper systems tend to result in higher yields from the owned portion
compared to the rented portion of the land.
6.        Another productivity constraint is the excessive fragmentation and
dispersion of land holdings caused by inheritance laws under which real pstate
is divided between heirs. More than half of the holdings are made up of six
or more parcels.
7.        Although agrarian reform in Turkey began in 1926, a land reform
law was passed only in July 1973. This law fixes maximum size limits for farms,
varying according to climatic and soil conditions. In November 1973 Urfa, a
province in southeastern Turkey, was selected as a pilot area for land reform
implementation and the expropriation of land started in November 1974. The
appointment of an Undersecretary for Land Reform in Mlay 1975 may indicate the
Government's increased interest in this field.
1/   N. Ramachandran:  Agricultural and Industrial Development Policies in
Turkey. A review, OECD, Paris 1974.



ANNEX 1
Page 4
Farming Techniques and Farm Inputs
8.        Compared to many other developing countries with similar levels of
GNP/capita, farming techniques in Turkey are rather modern, including the use
of tractors, combine harvesters and fertilizer (Tables 6 and 7). In 1973,
160,000 tractors were in use in Turkey, i.e. one tractor per 156 ha cultivated
land.  Turkey has one tractor factory and five assembling plants, with a 1974
production of 35,000 tractors. This production could be increased since these
plants operated at only 73% of capacity. In view of the strong demand for
tractors, resulting in a large black market. their use is expected to increase
at a rapid pace provided more roreign exchange is made available to import
prefabricated parts. The utilization of tractors seems to be relatively high
because custom work is common. Although the employment effects of tractors
are generallv negative, substantial yield increases can be observed. mainly
due to more timely and accurate land preparation.
9.        In 1973, about 10,000 combine harvesters were in use in Turkey,
most of them owned by contractors operating throughout the country. The
average area harvested per combine is estimated at 350 ha resulting in a total
area of 3.5 million ha, which is about 25% of the total grain area. Small and
remote fields are harvested by conventional methods.
10.       A portion of the fertilizer used in Turkey is being produced within
the country, as shown below.

Fertilizer use per ha of agricultural land (excluding fallow and pastures)
has averaged about 115 kg of nitrogen plus about 82 kg phosphate. Assuming
average N and P205 contents of 22% and 18%, respectively, the total fertil-
izer consumption can be regarded as reasonably high. However, due to lack of
credit, most of the fertilizer is being used by wealthier farmers in irrigated
agriculture, while most Central Anatolian farmers use only about half the
amount recommended. Fertilizer has been an important factor in the rapid
increases in productivity of cotton and sugarbeet over the past decade (paras
29 and 30 and Table 2) and to a lesser extent, it has also contributed to
higher cereal yields, mainly in the coastal. areas. In the marginal rainfall
areas, fertilizer has been introduced slowly since high yield increases can
only be expected in years with adequate precipitation.



ANNEX 1
Page 5
11.       The use of insecticides, herbicides, pesticides, and improved seed
is hampered by lack of credit, particularly in poorer regions such as in Central
Anatolia. Other constraints are the lack of technical advice for farmers and
the limited availability of improved seed.
B. Policies and Strategies for Agricultural Development
Agricultural Policy
12.       The Turkish Government exerts a strong influence on agriculture.
The main instruments used to achieve this are price policy for farm commodities
and inputs, export and import regulations, credit policy and publicly-financed
investments. Over the last two decades, agricultural policy has emphasized in-
creased production and exports, mainly through price structures and investments
in irrigation systems. While the overall growth rate of agriculture has been
satisfactory the price policy has failed to contribute significantly to welfare
goals. On the other hand, former policy accentuated regional disparities by
penalizing producers of crops with sharply fluctuating yields. This is
particularly true for wheat producers in Anatolia (para 27). This policy has
also discouraged productivity gains and shifts in production, especially to-
wards livestock and livestock feed.
Price Policy
13.       Forces of supply and demand play a limited role in determining
agricultural prices in Turkey, since many agricultural commodity prices are
under the comprehensive control of the Government. As of May, 1975 minimum
support prices apply for wheat, cotton, hazelnuts, tobacco, sugar beet,
raisins, dry figs, sunflower, tea, rice and, to a limited extent, for beef.
Direct intervention is not practiced for fruits and vegetables, livestock
feeds and milk, but, of course, their prices are influenced by intervention
in other product prices and by other policy measures. Minimum support prices
are specified annually (though frequently much too late to influence producers'
decisions) by a decree of the Council of Ministers. The decisions are based
on recommendations of the State Planning Organization (SPO) and the other
agencies involved. In recent years research institutes have been asked to
provide background information (such as production costs) in pricing various
commodities. However, the pricing decisions are strongly influenced by poli-
tical considerations. The respective agency (see below), is directed to buy
all available quantities at the floor price, and Government provides funds
to cover the operating losses of the agencies involved.
14.       Wheat prices are supported by the Soils Product Office (TMO), a
State Economic Enterprise (SEE), which purchases about 10% of total annual
cereal production and sells the wheat at a fixed (positive or negative) margin
to flour mills. TMO also handles all imports, storage and distribution of
cereals. The remaining 90% of cereal production is sold by farmers to the



ANNEX 1
Page 6
private sector at prices generally higher than those paid by TMO. Purchases
of sugarbeet are handled through the Sugar Factories Corporation (TSF), which
is a long-established and powerful SEE (Annex 5). It has a monopoly on sugar-
beet purchases, processing, retail sugar sales and imports. Turkish State
Monopolies are responsible for purchases and exports of tea and tobacco. The
Meat and Fish Organization (MFO) another SEE, buys about 10% of cattle, sheep
and goat production (Annex 5). Because of management problems and an alleged
lack of resources, its influence on the livestock sector is limited. Price
intervention for cotton, hazelnuts, dried figs, olive oil and sunflower is
handled by sales cooperatives. The Union of Cooperatives for each commodity
concerned acts as an agent for the Government by purchasing all commodities
offered at the minimum price from both members and non-members. For all
commodities mentioned, prices vary with quality. There are, however, no
regional variations in support prices except for cotton and tobacco.
15.       Ceiling consumer prices for certain food products, such as bread,
milk or meat, determined by municipal authorities in several big cities, are
another influence on farm prices. There are, however, wide variations between
different cities.
16.       The table below shows the development of major support prices for
commodities of average quality.

After a period of stagnation or moderate price increases in 1970-1972, prices
rose dramatically in 1973-1974. These increases, substantially above the 1973
and 1974 inflation rates (20-30%), have resulted in heavy subsidies and, in
the case of cotton, in stocks which had to be exported at a price well below
the purchase price.
17.       In general, the price control system has been implemented effective-
ly. Because of the high subsidies required for some commodities (such as
cotton in 1974), provision of sufficient funds has sometimes caused problems.
Although overall growth rates for agriculture have been reasonably good, the
pricing system has failed to achieve a balanced growth, with certain commodi-
ties, such as cotton, tea or hazelnuts benefiting from the pricing system, and



ANNEX 1
Page 7
others such as grain and livestock suffering from it. However, the future de-
velopment of agriculture will only partly depend on price policy for farm out-
puts. Input availability and prices, credit availability and provision of
an adequate extension service are equally important.
Farm InEut Prices
18.       Prices of tractors and fertilizer are also determined by Government
through the Farm Supply Organization (TZDK), an SEE. TZDK is responsible for
procurement (including imports) and distribution of both tractors and fer-
tilizer. It also operates one tractor assembly plant. Because of limited
availability, tractors and fertilizer, are not traded on free markets. Farm-
ers can buy those inputs from TZDK only if they meet specified requirements,
such as minimum areas of arable land. Physical distribution is being car-
ried out partly by TZDK itself, partly by other agencies, and if purchases
are on credit, by TCZB and its credit cooperatives. Except for the period
August, 1974 to April 1975, fertilizer prices were kept at low levels (Table
7B), e.g. farmers' purchase price of urea in April 1975 was at 80% of the CIF
price. Similarly, tractors are sold to farmers at relatively low prices
$4,500 for a locally assembled 40-50 hp tractor). As a consequence of low
prices combined with limited availability, black markets have developed for
both fertilizer and tractors with farmers paying up to 120% of the official
prices.
Enport and Import Regulations
19.       Because of continuous strains on the balance of payments, Turkey
exercises strict regulation of exports and imports. Exports of agricultural
commodities including processed foodstuffs are generally promoted by sub-
sidies, such as tax rebates and interest subsidies for exporters and manu-
facturers. Tax rebates vary between 10% and 30% of the export value depending
on the commodity concerned and the firm's export volume. Exporters' interest
rates on working capital are reduced by 3% and for investment capital by 6%.
On the other hand, Turkey imposes some restrictions on exports. To provide
an adequate domestic supply of basic consumer goods, exports are restricted
for several commodities (e.g., meat, beans and cheese). For other commodities
(e.g., hazelnuts and tomato paste), the Government has established minimum
export prices in order to maximize foreign exchange earnings. As a result
of such restrictions, export possibilities for some commodities (tomato paste)
could not be fully exploited, thus causing a negative effect on Turkey's for-
eign exchange earnings and a build-up of stocks.
20.       Imorts are also strictly regulated.  A complex system of duties
and taxes leads to high domestic prices for many consumer goods. Food imports
are mostly carried out by SEEs (see para 14) and subject to licenses issued by
the Ministry of Commerce.



ANNEX 1
Page 8
Credit Policy
21.       Through the Agricultural Bank (TCZB) 1/ the Turkish Government pro-
vides virtually all the institutional credit to agriculture, which is made
available to about 50-60% of all commercial farms. As a result of relatively
low interest rates for both deposits and loans compared to the prevailing in-
flation rate, credit supply cannot meet credit demand so that it needs to be
rationed. Interest rates that farmers have to pay are generally below those
for commercial credits (farm credits 11%,, agroindustries credits 11-14%O other
eommprrial rrei-itq 18-2291) 9/! although the administration cost for agricul-
tural credit is generally higher than for commercial loans.
Public Investments
22.       The Turkish Government has continuouslv provided funds for various
investments in agriculture (Table 11). Of these, irrigation is the largest
single category covering 30-50 of total investments. In view of the large
irrigation potential still to be realized in Turkey, this allocation seems
justified.
Agricultural Development Strategy
23.       The Third Five-Year Plan (1973-1977) aims at achieving the agricul-
tural growth rate of 4.5% p.a. through substantial fixed capital investments,
which would raise the sector's productivity. With this aim, the Plan provides
for an investment of TL 33 billion (12% of total) in agriculture, of which
53% would be in the public sector and the remaining 47% in the private sector.
The main development strategies under the plan are to; (a) induce investments
in projects with short gestation periods and low capital-output ratios, (b)
allocate credit for investments and inputs consistent with overall Plan ob-
jectives, (c) adopt a price policy which leaves incentives for farmers and
yet protects consumer interests through selective government intervention and
(d) focus public agricultural services such as research, extension and market-
ing along the stated priorities of the Plan.
24.       Projected agricultural growth rates during the Third Plan appear
optimistic in the light of the negative (-1.6%) rates for 1973 and 1974
brought about bv adverse weather conditions. However, despite the bumper
crop in 1975 and the expected 10% growth rate, annual values of 3% for the
Third Plan appear more realistic. Due to the tight world food situation and
the sharp decrease in foreign exchange reserves there will, nevertheless, be
even more Government emphasis on agriculture especially in the production of
livestock and exportable goods (such as fresh and processed fruits and vege-
tables) in order to improve the balance of payments.
1/   See Annex 2 and 3.
2/   In several cases interest rebates of up to 7% are being granted.  For
details see Annex 2, Table 1.



ANNEX 1
Page 9
Pro ect's Contribution to Agricultural Development
25.       The investments to be financed under the project are in line with
the Third Plan and the Government's latest priorities for agricultural devel-
opment. Specifically, the agroindustry component would contribute to increased
agricultural exports and induce larger production of fruits and vegetables.
The Supervised Credit Program (SCP) component would make more credit available
for on-farm investments and inputs with emphasis on grain production in
Central Anatolia. The livestock fattening element would benefit the less
developed eastern regions of Turkey and increase meat production and supplies
which are needed to satisfy a growing domestic demand. The project would
also generate sizeable non-quantifiable benefits such as expanding and
strengthening the technical support staff of TCZB, securing a more purposeful
coordination between agricultural research and extension, creating new entre-
preneurial and technical skills, improving existing financial feasibility
analysis methods, providing more efficient training programs, and improving
the organization of project agencies.
C. Production and Marketing
General Development of Agricultural Production
26.       In the 1950's, agricultural production grew almost exclusively as a
result of expansion in the crop area, as yields increased only slowly. After
1960, expansion of cultivated area and of livestock numbers slowed down marked-
ly and came almost to a standstill in the early 1970's. Cereals and livestock
have experienced the lowest productivity increases, and as a result, the share
of the central and eastern Anatolian regions in total production has declined.
Some statistical figures on production development are given below:

ANNEX 1
Page 10
Cereals
27.       Production-  Cereals account for about 75% of the area sown and
40% of the value added by crop production. WIheat covers 67% of the area
planted to cereals. Over the last decade, Turkey's cereal production has
fluctuated greatly (table 2), mainly due to varving yields in rainfed agri-
culture. Increases in wheat production from 1961 to 1973 can be mainly
attributed to a large expansion of the wheat area. Yields increased only
moderately until 1971 and dropped sharply thereafter due to unfavorable
weather conditions. Because of a lack of competitive crops in rainfed areas.
the land planted to cereals is not heavily dependent on price changes. Gov-
ernment's recent increase in wheat buying prices (para 14) coupled with re-
duced fertilizer costs (para 18) could however lead to the desired intensi-
fication of wheat production, especially in Central Anatolia. If present
input/output price relations were maintained for some years, the Plan's tar-
get for raising national wheat yield averages from 1.2 to 1.6 tons/ha could
be achieved. Although wheat yield fluctuations are expected increase from
a range of 0.8 to 1.6 tons/ha to 1.0 to 2.2 tons/ha, thereby increasing
farmers risks, the proposed project would increase Turkey's cereal production
by providing credit for integrated farm development, fertilizer and other
modern farm inputs.
28.       Marketing:  In recent years, Turkey has not been able to export
grain, with the exception of barley (Table 8), and there have been some wheat
imDorts in years of poor harvest. In 1974, 1.5 million tons of wheat were
imported comprising about 6% of the country's total import expenditures. In
view of the rapid population growth and the shortage of new crop land which
could be planted to wheat, Turkey will find it difficult to increase wheat
supply commensurate with the proected demand.  The anticipated supply gap
for barley and other feed Ggrains is even larger than for wheat since, with
growing per capita income, livestock feed requirements will increase rapidly.
Therefore, no marketing problems are anticipated for the project's incremental
cereal production which would amount to only 0.2% of Turkey's 1974 wheat and
barley production and which would be used entirely for import substitution.
Industrial Crops and Oilseeds
29.       Production-  Cotton, sunflower, sugarbeet, tobacco (and others)
cover a large portion of Turkey's most fertile and mainly irrigated land (see
Table 2). Further yield increases are possible, provided additional ferti-
lizer and other inputs are used. Most of the cotton competes with high-
yielding wheat and vegetables. Farmers are profit-oriented and respond
quickly to price changes with the result, that in 1974 because of the rise
in cotton support price they produced a heavy surplus (para 16).
30.       Sugarbeet and Sunflower   Production grew rapidly during the last
decade (Table 2). For sugarbeet this was due mainly to a 50% yield increase
resulting from effective agricultural extension and credit services, while
sunflower output quadrupled, aainly in line with area expansion. TSF has



AMNEX 1
Page 11
recently assumed responsibility for sunflower production in sugarbeet areas.
Future sugarbeet production which TSF can manipulate through acreage allot-
ment is expected to grow at a slower pace. Production of sunflower, other
oilseeds and industrial crops is also expected to increase further. Invest-
ment and input credit under the proposed project is likely to contribute to
the expected production increase, especially in non-sugarbeet areas where in-
sufficient credit has been a constraint.
31.       Marketing:  A high percentage of cotton (72% in 1973) used to be
exported. Since 1974, however Turkey's cotton industry has been facing severe
export problems because;
(a) world market prices have dropped dramatically and;
(b) Turkey has experienced inflation rates higher than world
inflation and there has been no corresponding devaluation
of the Turkish Lira.
As a consequence, Turkey's cotton producers have difficulty competing with
farmers in countries with relatively lower inflation. The 1974 cotton stocks
were partly sold at low prices in early 1975. To avoid high storage costs
and subsidies to wealthy farmers, the Government will have to be careful in
determining future support prices. Although no crop is explicitly excluded
from SCP credits, the proposed project is not expected to have a substantial
impact on cottor. output.
32.       Sugar consumption has been growing at a rapid rate necessitating
some sugar imports in 1975 and incremental sugarbeet production would be for
import substitution. Sunflower is processed generally in small oilmills for
the rapidly increasing local consumption. Approximately 83% of the tobacco
production was exported, accounting for 10% of Turkey's 1973 total export
earnings.
Vegetables, Fruits and Nuts
33.       Production.  Turkey, with its climate, soil and irrigation resources,
has potential for expanding both fruit and vegetable production, however, the
industry is still in an early stage of development. The total vegetable pro-
duction area increased from 416,000 ha in 1968 to 530,000 ha in 1973 (Table 1),
comprising a substantial part of the total irrigated area. More detailed in-
formation on production 1/ is given in Table 4. Melons and watermelons rank
first in quantity though production has stagnated at 3.5 million tons. In
contrast, tomato production increased rrapidly from 1.3 to 2 million tons,
largely through improvements in yield. Production of other vegetables, except
green beans, pepper and cauliflower, has grown more slowlv.
1/ No information about areas and yields is available.



ANNEX 1
Page 12
34.       The proposed project would help increase vegetable production, es-
pecially by using greenhouses and plastic tunnels for early vegetable cultiva-
tion mainly in the Mediterranean region. Because of the mild climate of this
region, harvest time for early vegetables is sooner than for those grown in
most parts of Italy and Spain, giving Turkey a substantial comparative ad-
vantage. (For detailed data, see farm models in Annex 3, Table 7).
35.       The area planted to fruit and olive trees and vineyards has been
expanded by about 50% since 1961 and most of the increase was in orchards.
Although grape production ranks first, there has been almost no growth
sincee9   (Table 3).  In contrast, apple production, which ranks second,
has experienced continuously high growth rates, while growth in citrus
production was also high until 1968. However, production almost stagnated
thereafter. Yields per tree are lower than those of competitive countries and
efforts are being made under the Fresh Fruit and Vegetable Export Project
TU 762/257 to increase yields and improve quality. Turkey, as the world's
largest exporter of hazelnuts, sells about 38% of its annual production abroad.
Olive oil is a major export commodity with about 50% of total production being
sold abraod. Nuts and olive oil production, however, are characterized by heavy
yearly fluctuations in yield (Table 3). Although productivity has increased
there is still scope for further growth through improved management and tech-
nical and credit services which wotuld be provided under the project.
36.       At full development, the project would increase Turkey's citrus
productionl by 0.6% and tomato and cucumber production by 0.2%. However, in
value terms the latter figure would be much higher since prices for early
vegetables grown in greenhouses and plastic tunnels are two to three times
higher than conventionally grown vegetables which are marketed later.
37.       The contemplated investments for storing, cooling and processing of
fruits and vegetables would have a significant impact on Turkey's present
processing capacities and raw material requirements:

38.       Marketing.  Turkey with relatively high income elasticities of
demand (0.6 for vegetables and 0.8 for fruits) consumed in 1973, about 85 kg
of grapes, 20 kg of apples and 15 kg of citrus products per person. However,
per capita consumption of vegetables followed the fluctuations in production
varying between 90 and 150 kg. Since most fruits and vegetables have been
consumed fresh, processing, storing and cooling of fruits and vegetables have
been of minor importance. However, during the 1970s, with the construction
of tomato paste and juice concentrate plants, production of tomato paste,
citrus and grape juice concentrates has increased to where 18, 36 and 10%
respectively of the domestically produced raw materials can be processed.
39.       Commercially operated refrigerated cooling and storing facilities
have been practically nonexistent in Turkey in the past. However, five cold
stores and fruit packing houses being financed under the Fruit and Vegetable
Export Project L/C 762/257 TU are expected to become operational in 1976.
40.       Unlike the situation with nuts and olives (see paragraph 35) ex-
ports of fresh fruits and vegetables have been erratic and for some commodi-
ties such as grapes, have been declining during the early 1970s (see Table 10).
The 1972 and 1973 exports of some fresh commodities as percentages of the
respective total production are shown below:
Exports as % of Total Production
1972                         1973
Lemons                      38                           64
Oranges and Tangerines        7                           6
Grapes                       0.4                          0.3
Apples                        0                           0.2
Onions                        3                           3
Tomatoes                     0                             1
Hazelnuts                   50                           46
41.       The decline and/or stagnation of fruit and vegetable exports has
been attributed to a number of causes including:
(a) The lack of cold storage and packing facilities.
(b) Until recently, the fleet of refrigerated trucks and trailers
was too small to transport sizeable quantities of fresh fruits
and vegetables to Europe and the Middle East. However, the
situation has improved with the procurement of 155 trailers in
1974 under the L/C 762/257 TU.
(c) Since 1973, Turkey has experienced substantially higher inflation
rates than those prevailing internationally. As devaluations of
the Turkish Lira have not kept pace with inflation, exporters
have been facing difficulties competing with other suppliers.



ANNEX 1
Page 14
(d) Turkey's exports to the EEC are subject to higher duties than
those from some other non-EEC suppliers, although the Government
has compensated by granting rebates up to 30% of the market
value to exporters and processors.
(e) Increased domestic demand with corresponding high prices competing
for fresh fruits and vegetables.
(f) The inability of Turkish exporters to adjust the overall quantities
offered in particular markets as a result of sharply varying
price situations. The lack of homogenous quality, standardization,
brand promotion and representation needed to compete with other
supplying countries.
42.       The incremental fruit and vegetable production of the proposed proj-
ect to be financed under the SCP is estimated to be about 3,900 tons of citrus
and 5,500 tons of early vegetables. About 25% of this production would be
exported and the balance would be absorbed by the increasing domestic demand.
A major portion of the proposed investments in Agro-Industries would be for
facilities to cool and store fresh fruits, and these facilities, in addition
to those for storage of about 5,000 tons of apples at the farm level provided
under the SCP project, would make significant quantities of apples available
to meet the increased demand of Iran and the Arab countries. The proposed
RO/RO ferryboat would increase transport capacity and reduce the dependence
on more costly over-the-road transport (Annex 6). The incremental juice
concentrate production (2,600 tons citrus and 5,900 tons grape and other
fruit juices) would be mainly for export. Market prospects appear favoarable
both in Europe and several Arab countries.
43.       It is expected that of the incremental olive oil production gener-
at,ed by the proposed project, about 50% of Grade A oil will be exported with
the balance of the Grade A and all of the Grade B oil being consumed locally.
In view of the high growth rates of both population and income, market prospects
appear to be good.
Livestock
44.       Production.  Annual rates of growth of livestock products were 2.6%
and 3.2% for the 1st and 2nd Five Year Plans, respectively compared to the
targeted 5.6% and 4.8% (3rd Plan estimates of 5% are also not being achieved).
Some reasons for this inadequate supply lie in the poor availability and high
costs of feedstuffs, which combined with demand rising faster than supply led
to shortages and rapid increases in prices of livestock and livestock products.1/
45.       Cattle numbers are expected to rise to 13.8 million by 1978, a rise
of 9% from 1971. Dairy cows are expected to increase by about 15% over the
same period but not to compensate for the forecasted fall-in beef cow nunbers.
1/   Economic Development of Turkey Vol. III, 1974 IBRD.



ANNEX 1
Page 15
These figures reflect a switch to dual-purpose breeds and the development of
milk processing facilities.
46.       The principal cattle and sheep rearing regions are in the eastern
provinces (North East particularly), sheep being raised for milk as well as
for mutton and wool. Overgrazing and the ensuing undernutrition of livestock
is reflected in low dressed carcass weights (Annex 5). Turkish beef produc-
tion aims to increase the rate of removal of animals from pastures, most of
which are communally-grazed. These animals would be fattened on forage and/or
crop residual-based diets to produce a live animal of over 200 kg after an
intensive feeding period. A major emphasis is being placed by Government,
through the State Planning Office, on improving livestock production. This
is reflected in the third 5-year plan and in recent price regulations im-
plemented to satisfy both consumer and producer.
47.       It is difficult to determine the relative effect of the increase
on total beef and veal production as statistics vary considerably depending
on the source. It is estimated that inspected slaughterhouses handle one-half
of total domestic production (97,000 tons of beef). Therefore, the expected
12,500 ton of beef resulting from this project would represent an increase of
6% over the present estimated total of 200,000 tons (Annexes 3 and 5). Feed
mills needed to complement the beef fattening component in the eastern pro-
vinces are also envisaged under the project (Annex 4).
48.       Production of whole milk (excluding milk suckled) has increased
steadily since 1961. With the marked increase expected in milk yields per
cow and the increase in the number of dairy cow, whole milk production by
1978 is expected to rise by 43% over 1971 with the largest increases occurring
in the years 1976-78.
49.       Poultry numbers have increased from 29 million in 1965 to 37 mil-
lion in 1973 with an increase in poultry meat and egg production.  However
the relatively high income elasticity of demand for poultry show that it is
still considered a luxury item.  Poultry has also been affected more than
the other livestock sectors by the rapid rise in feedstuff prices.  The Bank
project (SCP, Annex 3) could increase production of eggs by 2% (50 million)
over 1973 values.
50.       Marketing.  The income elasticitv of demand for meat in Turkev is
0.93, consisting of beef and veal 0.8, mutton and lamb 1.0 and poultry 1.5.1/
In recent years Turkev has passed from a net exporter of live cattle, beef and
veal (12,000 tons carcass equivalent annuallv from 1969 to 1971) to a net im-
porter in 1973 (1,000 tons carcass equivalent). However domestic consumption
has traditionally absorbed almost all production and this situation is not
expected to change in the foreseeable future. The demand for live animals
for slaughter in the Moslem countries to the east and south of Turkey has
traditionally resulted in active often illegal exports. The desired expansion
1/   FAO "Agricultural Commodity Projections 1970-80"'.



ANNEX 1
Page 16
of beef and poultry production for domestic consumption could increase the in-
take of animal protein in the diet. In 1974, estimates of per capita total
meat consumption in the villages and rural areas were 13.5 kg/year of (of
which 3.7 kg was beef) and 20 kg/year in the cities (of which 7.0 kg was beef).
51.       Traditional livestock marketing practices have not improved the
supply-demand problem. A large proportion of cattle are still slaughtered
off pasture in the earlv fall due to lack of feed and credit for over-
wintering, thus resulting in low carcass weights. Inadequate slaughtering
and cold storage facilities often result in long delays for the producer
before his animals can be slaughtered and this often requires unscheduled
feeding or actual weight loss. The two slaughterhouses and cold storage
facilities envisaged under this project (Annex 4) would alleviate this sit-
uation to a certain extent.
52.       The above traditional marketing difficulties resulted in severe
seasonal fluctuations in producer prices for cattle until recent Government
price support was implemented, with only partial success, in 1974. These
problems are often compounded by the large distances between the areas of
production in the East and the large population centers of Ankara and the
West. The transportation of live animals often results in up to a 10%,' weight
loss en route. However, the very large reservoir of livestock in the east has
acted as a supply buffer to these market vagaries over the long-term and this
will form the basis of the predictable expansion of meat production in Turkey.
53.       Cattle fattened under this project would be slaughtered by the
Government-owned Meat and Fish Organization (MFO), 1/ which handles about 10%
of live cattle and sheep slaughterings in Turkey. The remainder are channelled
through private or municipal premises. The MFO marketing arrangement involves
contractual agreements (Annex 5), guaranteed minimum prices and fixed dates of
slaughter to the cattle fattener. The farmer does have flexibility to market
his animals elsewhere, although future contracts would be difficult to nego-
tiate.

APPRAISAL OF AN
AGRICULTURAL CREDIT AND AGROINDUSTRIES PROJECT
TURKEY
The Agricultural Bank of Turkey
A. Organization and Structure
Legal Background
1.        TC Ziraat Bankasi (TCZB), the Agricultural Bank of Turkey, is the
largest credit institution in the country and fully owned by the Turkish
Government.  Besides the Central Bank and TCZB there are 41 other banks and
financial agencies in Turkey, including:
10 banks founded by special laws (as TCZB);
18 private commercial banks operating all over Turkey;
6 regional commercial banks;
5 foreign commercial banks; and
2 investment banks, the Industrial Development Bank of Turkey and
the Bank of Industrial Investment and Credit.
TCZB accounts for more than one-fourth of the total deposits of Turkey's
banking system and provides over 90% of the institutional credit to agricul-
ture.
2.        TCZB dates back to 1863, but was established in its present form
in 1937. It operates as a commercial bank, engaging in all types of banking
transactions, but its portfolio is specialized in agricultural lending; that
is, encouraging agricultural production and marketing, helping farmers in
the acquisition of land, lending to and investing in agroindustries, making
commercial loans, and extending credits to agricultural credit and marketing
cooperatives which are unique to Turkey in their operation and organization,
since they are closely related tco and under the supervision of TCZB.
3.        As a "State Economic Enterprise" (SEE), TCZB is under the jurisdic-
tion of the Ministry of Commerce and hence not free to determine its own salary
structure or bases for staff promotions.  However, it has a degree of autonomy
of action and is a profit-making organization. Its authorized capital is
TL 1,500 million as against a paid-up capital of TL 1,172 million on December
31, 1974. The Turkish Government has provided capital contributions of TL 785
million since 1937; additional TL 357 million after-tax profits of TCZB were
transferred to the paid-up capital during the past 37 years.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
4.        TCZB's affairs are controlled and regulated by the General Meeting,
the Hligher Committee of Credit Regulation, the Auditors, the Board of Direc-
tors, the Managing Committee, and the General Mlanagement.
5.        Law No. 468 of 1964 established the General Meeting, composed of 15
senators and 35 members of the House of Representatives, to review the opera-
tions and annual reports of SEEs. The General Meeting's report is submitted
first to the Senate, and then to the House of Representatives for discussion
and subsequernt approval or reject-ion.  Approval absolves the Board of Direc-
tors from operational and accounting responsibility for the year examined.
Owing to time pressures and other priorities, the Turkish Parliament has not
acted on TCZB statements since 1967.
6.        TCZB's Higher Committee of Credit Regulation consists of the
Ministers of Commerce (Chairman), Finance, and Agriculture, and the Chairman
of the Board of Directors (who is also General Manager of TCZB). This Commit-
tee is responsible for establishing TCZB's overall policies and the annual
investment program, including its distribution by sectors, types of credit,
etc.
7.        The General Manager, as noted above, is also Chairman of TCZB's
Board of Directors, and two Assistant General Managers are Board members.
Two additional members are appointed by the Council of 1.Ministers upon the
recommendation of the Ministers of Finance and Commerce. Another member is
elected by TCZB's client agricultural credit cooperatives. The six members
of the Board of Directors -- all with voting rights -- are selected from
among persons trained and experienced in economics, finance, and law.
8.        The Board approves the investment and loan allocation budget pro-
posed each year. It is also responsible for setting higher bank policy, re-
gulating the flow of agricultural credit, establishing lending criteria, and
authorizing loan approvals in excess of TL 150,000. 1/
Auditing and Control
9.        Statutory external audit of TCZB is carried out by the High Control
Board (HCB), which is responsible to the National Assembly through the Prime
Minister's Office.  As provided by Article 61 of the Banking Law, Miiinistry of
Finance examiners also scrutinize TCZB's activities in the head Office as well
as its branches, mainly to determine whether Banking Law provisions are being
respected. The Central Bank exerts its control by means of regular reports
required of TCZB on loan portfolio, deposits, foreign exchange transactions,
etc.
1/   According to Law No. 3202 of 1937 (TCZB Law), the limit was TL 200,000.
However, the banking law of 1958 requests the Board's approval for all
loans exceeding TL 150,000.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
10.       TCZB's Board of Inspectors and the Control Division are responsible
for internal auditing and control. The Board of Inspectors, which comprised
115 members in May 1975, engages in routine inspections of the 855 branches
and in investigations on special request. In 1974, 491 normal inspections
and 556 special investigations were carried out, in 1973 the figures were 510
and 727, respectively. Big branches are inspected in principle every year,
and small branches at least every third year.
11.       The staff of the Control Division (125 professionals) is concentrat-
ing on formal controls of credit transactions. About 100 controllers are per-
manently attached to the 14 biggest branches. The controllers in the Head
Office base their control on the monthly or quarterly lists and reports by
wlhich most of the branches give evidence of their credit operations.
Organizational Set-Up
12.       The General Manager and Eour Assistant General Managers are ap-
pointed by the Council of Ministers upon recommendation of the Minister of
Commerce. They direct TCZB's Ankara Hlead Office, organized in 32 divisions,
and a network of 855 branches providing nationwide coverage. Total banking
and specialized staff numbered 17,738 as of March 31, 1975.
13.       The branch managers report to the Head Office divisions concerned
(the latter acting on behalf of the General Management), and not directly to
the General Manager as the organigram (see annex chart) may suggest. There
is a limited decentralization of decision-making on a regional basis with
regard to loan approval authority of branch managers. Also, main branches in
big cities perform certain services for smaller branches, such as supplying
and receiving surplus physical cash and enforcing certain controls on behalf
of the Head Office.  All inter-branch transactions are handled through Head
Office accounts.
14.       The organigram in the annex chart may be changed after the position
of the Assistant General Manager B, vacant for several months, is filled. For
the time being certain functions, which might logically be assumed to be inter-
related, are in fact dispersed under different Assistant General Managers.
For example, the various credit divisions are spread under two Assistant
General Managers and the Secretary General. The Training Division and the
Staff Division are likewise divided. The Planning Division is only temporar-
i:Ly reporting to the same Assistant General Manager as the Budget and Program
Division, since the position of one Assistant General M4anager is vacant.
Accounting Procedures
15.       A large portion of TCZB's accounting is still done in the tradi-
tional manner, accounts being maintained in bound and often voluminous books.
Copying and transfer of totals and/or balances from the journal to individual



ANNEX 2
Page 4
accounts and to the ledger is niostly done manually. As a consequence, the
volume of work and staff requirements are enormous. And yet the information
offered by the accounting system is limited.
16.       In view of these insufficiencies, TCZB is modernizing its accounting
system and procedures. A decimal system of account classification has been
adopted. Accounting machines have been introduced in 57 of 855 branches for
deposit (but not yet for loan) accounts. A computer has been in use in the
Head Office since 1971, mainly for recording transactions within the network
of branches, monthly and quarterly balances of the branches' ledger accounts,
and various statistics. However, more and better use could be made of the
numerous staff employed (350 people in the Accounting and Computer Divisions
of the Head Office), of the existing technical facilities, and of the possi-
bilities offered by modern data processing.

17.       TCZB's resource structure as of December 31, 1973 and 1974 was as

Deposits
18.       Deposits constitute the most important capital resource, and have
increased steadily in absolute terms (although declining as proportion of
resources, since 1973) even though interest rates offered for deposits are
unattractive in view of an inflation rate of between 20% and 30%.
19.       Interest rates for deposits are fixed by the Government.  In May
1975 their structure was as follows:

20.        These rates are obligatory for all banks operating in Turkey.  The
rate for time deposits for more than 48 months (12%) may be fixed by a bank
up to 14%. However, according to an agreement within the Banking Union, banks
have decided to make no use of this possibility, and as a state-owned bank
TCZB respects this agreement.  Moreover, TCZB's on-lending rates, fixed by
the Government relatively low, would not allow for a sufficient spread if
TCZB paid deposit interests of more than 12%.

TCZB, and since 1973 have constituted the largest incremental resource. Most
of the rediscounts have been used to refinance loans to agricultural coopera-

marketing cooperatives, as seen from the following breakdown of year-end 1974

23.       The Higher Committee for Credit Regulation fixes the volume of
credits to be granted yearly by TCZB as well as the limits for the main credit
groups to be refinanced by the Central Bank, taking into account the pre-
vailing monetary policy, government's policies for financing of agricultural
operations through cooperatives and TCZB's needs. All kinds of short-term
credits may be refinanced up to these limits. For medium- and long-term
credits to be refinanced, the consent of the Central Bank is required on a
case-by-case basis.

25.       Aside from the above rediscount facilities, the Central Bank, under
the national priorities attached to agricultural development, may lend to
TCZB up to 20% of the statutory deposits maintained by all Turkish banks with
the Central Bank at 8% interest. After being under TL 700 million for a few
years, the level of such borrowings by TCZB had shot up rapidly in 1975, and
lhad practically reached the limit of TL 4 billion by October. The funds were
being used for lending to farmers, mainly for fertilizers, and were repayable
to the Central Bank in two years. There was a possibility of a later review
bY the Central Bank, to permit TCZB to retain some of these funds for speci-
fied longer terms.
Capital Subscription
26.       The Government is to contribute funds to TCZB in the form of capital
subscriptions in accordance with several provisions under law. Article 5 of
the TCZB Law provides for Government contributions to authorized capital (TL
1,500 million) of 1% of the aggregate estimated receipts scheduled in the
State's general budget. One percent of Government's estimated receipts from
1965 through 1974 amounted to TL :3,234 million; against this, the Government
actually contributed TL 134 million, the last such contribution having been
made in 1969. With the Government's tight budgetary situation, the above
Article 5 is likely to continue to be inoperative in future; the Government
will make its contributions in other ways.
27.       TCZB receives Government contributions according to various laws
providing for financial support from TCZB in connection with specific Govern-
ment supporting programs. TCZB was also entitled to receive 6% of land tax
revenues as Government capital contribution.  However, this provision resulted
in substantial contributions only up to 1971.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
28.       About one-third of capital subscriptions have come from transfers
of TCZB's profits, which require decisions of the Parliament. The last such
transfer was made in 1970, with effect on profits accrued up to 1966. Profits
accrued since 1967 are awaiting Parliament's decision. The total of accrued
net profits for the years from 1967 through 1974 amounts to about TL 570 mil-
lion. This amount increases TCZB's net worth at the close of 1974 from TL
1,508 million to TL 2,078 million.
Improved Resource Mobilization
29.       The Government and TCZB are aware of the need to increase TCZB's
capital. The Treasury has prepared a proposal for the Parliament, whereby
the authorized capital of SEEs could be increased by Cabinet decrees, with
actual increases effected through Budget law authorizations to the Treasury.
Once this is passed as a law, the capital of TCZB (one of the largest SEEs)
could be increased by Cabinet decree without the need for amending the TCZB
Act. In conjunction with the above, TCZB had drafted a proposal for increasing
its authorized capital from TL 1.5 billion to TL 5 billion, which is being
processed by the Government. Previous efforts to increase the capital of SEEs
through Budget laws had been frustrated by decisions of higher Courts, but the
proposed legislation is expected to overcome this obstacle. The need for
increased capital has become pressing for most SEEs, and the Government is
giving this matter high priority for legislation. If this law is passed, the
Government expects that the proce3s of transfer of TCZB's accrued net prorits
to capital will also be simplified.
30.       The controlled credit rate structure prevents state credit institu-
tions from mobilizing private savings on a meaningful scale. TCZB has had
moderate success ip attracting private deposits. However, its vast potential
in this field remains largely unrealized. The potential exists in two forms,
viz TCZB's network of over 850 branches, and its more than 2,000 client credit
cooperatives with over 1.3 million members. It exercises considerable control
over the management and operation of these societies, and uses them as a
primary channel for the provision of short-term credit to Turkish farmers.
However, very few of these cooperatives provide any financial services beyond
the extension of credit.
31.       Better credit decision-making could strengthen TCZB's portfolio,
permitting better forecasting of funds flows and diminishing the probability
of funds being tied up in overdue loans. Attention to timely collection sup-
ported by a more fully computerized accounting, could likewise free resources
for lending. An additional source of funds could be tapped by TCZB soliciting
deposits from its borrowers; at present such deposits are negligible.



ANNEX 2
Page 9
C. Lending Policies and Procedures
Allocation of Lending Resources
32.       Resources for lending are rationed according to the annual program
approved by the Higher Committee on Credit Regulation. There is no long-term
planning. The recommendations submitted to this Committee are presented by
TCZB's management. A basic requirement is that at least 80% of TCZB's lend-
ing be to agriculture. Within this limit, funds are rationed on the basis of
present performance and loan demand projections. For normal agricultural
credit, resources are allocated among branch offices on the basis of the
branch managers' yearly reports indicating the area of agricultural land
classified by land types and crop production in the branch territory, agri-
cultural development potential, number of livestock, and number of potential
borrowers and their estimated credit requirements.
Delegation of Loan Approval Authority
33.       The major limitation on the decentralization of loan decision-
making is the legal requirement in Turkey that loans in excess of TL150,000
(US$10,714) must be approved by a bank's board of directors. This requirement
is rigidly adhered to by TCZB and not interpreted to mean simply ex-post
board review and approval of commitments already made by management. Given
that members of senior management comprise half of TCZB's Board and that the
Board meets several times a month, this requirement per se does not materially
delay decisions. However, the system results in many loans being approved
above the branch level and hence involves delays caused by branch managers'
need to refer to higher levels in the administration. Inflation has, in
effect, reduced the degree of branch lending autonomy since the TL 150,000
limit was established in the 1950's, and it is logical to conclude that the
increase in the proportion of loans which require Board approval diminishes
the time the Board has available to devote to policy matters. Below the
TL 150,000 level, management's discretionary limits are scaled down from the
division chief level, provincial main branch level, first-class branch level
and second-class branch level. The pattern of lending limit distribution
varies according to loan types, but managers of second-class branches have
limits of TL 50,000 (US$3,571) or more for most types of facilities.
Loan Appraisal
34.       Agricultural loans are primarily appraised by assessing the value
of collateral offered by borrowers and their past repayment records. These
loans are referred to the local agents of the Ministry of Agriculture for
opinion and guidance. These agents play an important role in determining
farmers' short-term credit requirements. The field technicians actually check
farmers' requests for loans in kind, such as those for seeds, fertilizer, and
fuel. For medium- and long-term borrowers, they may assist in working out
loan projects in liaison with thie TCZB branch office. Forms for various types



ANNEX 2
Page 10
of investment loans are signed by the local representatives of the Ministry
of Agriculture and the representative of the branch. The forms, properly
filled out, provide the basic data and information to make loan decisions.
AA far as supervised Credit Program (SCP) loans are concerned, TCZB's super-
visors function in a similar way to the agents of the Ministry of Agriculture
(Detailed information on SCP is given in Annex 3).
35.       A Project Evaluation Section was established in 1969 in the En-
couragement and Development Division of the Head Office, to appraise loans
exceeding TL 500,000 (agroindustrial and farm development projects) or TL
100,000 (SCP loans). The Project Evaluation Section had a staff of 31 in
May 1975, of whom 23 were civil, mechanical and agricultural engineers, agro-
economists and economists. Recent project evaluation reports prepared by the
Section show that efforts are being made to apply modern methods of technical,
economic and financial appraisal. However, the need to improve evaluation
skills is obvious, not only in the Project Evaluation Section. Technical
assistance could speed up the necessary improvements.
Basis for Computing the Size of Agricultural Loans
36.        Except for supervised agricultural credit and Topraksu (land level-
ing and irrigation) loans which are based on the appraisal of farm projects,
short-term loans for farm operations are based on TCZB's standard credit limits
(in terms of fixed amount per decare under individual crops) and the land
area that the farmer-applicant proposes to devote to such crops. Marketing
loans are based on the weight of the produce to be sold (e.g. tobacco: TL 3
to 6 per kg).
37.        Seasonal operating loans are disbursed in cash and in kind, largely
through cooperatives closely affiliated with TCZB. Seeds, fertilizers and
fuel are given in kind in specified volumes per unit (decare) of land; cash
is provided in addition for pesticides, labor and other out-of-pocket costs.
Maximum per decare cash limits for a broad range of crops are as follows:

T'CZB's Lending Rate Structure
38.       The complex structure of interest rates charged by TCZB on loans
and the rates actually paid by borrowers are shown in Table 1. To TCZB's
interest is added, in most cases, an annual commission. TCZB's interest
rates are equal to or less than the ceilings set by the Government from time
to time, which vary according to the sector financed, loan purpose, (including
export prospects) and location of borrower. The difference between TCZB's
lending rate plus commission and the cost of funds to borrowers arises from a
system of levies by the Government (usually, a 25% financial transfer tax;
also mortgage charges and stamp duties as applicable) and selective rebates
made by Government. Therefore the cost of capital to borrowers is generally
higher than interest paid to TCZB, unless reduced sufficiently by the selective
rebates. In 1975 lending interest rates of TCZB ranged from 8.4% to 14%, plus
annual commissions in certain cases; the cost to borrowers ranged from 8% to
14.125%. Interest on regular agricultural loans was 10.5% plus 0.5% commission.
Types of Agricultural Loans
=39.      In the past five years TCZB's agricultural loans have been dominated
by lending to agricultural cooperatives, which grew especially since 1973, as
shown in Table 2. Loans to agricultural marketing cooperatives (closely af-
filiated with and supervised by TCZB) accounted for 61% of total loans out-
standing at the end of 1974, loans to credit cooperatives (likewise linked to
TCZB, for onlending to members primarily for seasonal operating purposes)
for 10% and normal annual agricultural production loans for 21%. Of the
remainder, less than 3% constituted supervised credit, and less than 0.5%
"encouragement and development" loans, including medium-and long-term loans
f'or purchase of equipment and livestock.
Repayment Schedules
40.       Repayment of medium- and long-term loans is scheduled in equal in-
stalments on principal, thus resulting in declining interest charges on
amounts outstanding and in declining annuities. Thus, the borrower faces the
highest burden of debt servicing during the first years, when the investment
is at best just starting to render incremental income. In later years, when
the operations financed by the loan are expected to make progress and to
improve the borrower's financial capacity, the loan requires only declining
annuities. A shift to equal annuities would be more in line with the borrower's
repayment capacity and could contribute to improved loan collection.
Loan Collection
41.       The extent of overdue loans signals tnat defaults on, or late re-
payment of, agricultural loans are serious problems of Turkey's agricultural
credit system (see Section D). The fast growing volume of TCZB's loan port-
f'olio and of the amounts in arrears indicate the necessity for an improved

loan collection. The amounts of loans not collected compared to loans that
should have been collected vary considerably for the different kinds of loans.

42.       Normally, first notice on a loan or an instalment falling due is
given by the bank branch concerned one month before the due date, either
directly to the borrower or through the Muhtar (vilIlage chief). A second
notice is given after the due date, asking for repayment within 30 days.



ANNEX 2
Page 13
43.       Loans are classified as overdue shortly after their due date. 1/
If loans must be paid back in several instalments and one installment is not
collected when due, the total loan outstanding (including installments not
yet due according to the repayment schedule) is considered overdue. Small
amounts of loans are periodicallv rescheduled 2/ - TL 126 millior and TL 77
mlillion in 1973 and 1974, respectively.
44.       Legal action is initiated when loans are more than one year overdue
o,r when other reasons reguire it (for example, dishonesty of the borrower).
Legal actiJW. means the initiation of procedures suci as a formal request to
t:he borrower to repay, and informing the legal authorities if the borrower is
unresponsive.  In most cases an arrangement between TCZPE and the delinquent
borrower is reached.  Only exceptionally will TCZB sell securities, especially
if land has been mortgaged.
45.       As of the end of 1974, over half or 52% of TCZB's overdue loans were
accounted for by loans to agricultural marketing cooperatives for financing
purchases against Government price support schemes, while another 38% was on
account of annual agricultural producticn loans. Over 18 of total loans out-
Standing were affected by arrearages.  Mlost of TCZB's lending to cooperatives
is Government-directed or on account of Government sponsored programs.  In
accordance with a law adooted in 'ay 1975, which is applicable to certain
public enterprises, part of TCZB's debts to the Central Bank on account of
loans to cooperatives have been consolidated and taken over by the Treasury
as of December 31, 1974, thus ameliorating the consequences of overdues. The
amount involved was TL 3.9 billion. consisting of TL 1.7 billion owed to TCZB
by marketing cooperatives and TL 2.2 billion by credit cooperatives.
D.  Financial Analysis of TCZB, 1964 - 1974
46.       The overall pattern of TCZB's operations during the 11 years under
review has been one of steady growth. Many balance sheet and income statement
relationships have remained relatively stable, one of the exceptions being
net worth, which has not grown commensurate with TCZR's total assets.
1/   Agricultural loans are considered overdue on the first day of the month
following the due date. Other loans are classified as overdue on the
next working day after falling due.
2/   Rescheduling means changes in scheduled repayment dates and reclassifying
of overdue loans as loans not yet due, after an agreement between TCZB
and the borrowers.



ANNEX 2
Page 14
Balance Sheet
47.       Although capital and reserves (net worth, Table 3, Item 1.04) grew
by about 64% over the period under review, total liabilities plus equity in-
creased seven-fold (Table 3, Item 3.01). As a result, TCZB's debt/equity
ratio increased from about 6:1 to 23:1 between 1964 and 1974, if accrued net
profits for those years amounting to TL 570 million are included in the equity
for 1974.
48.       As the major lender to agriculture, a priority sector, TCZB has
access to massive rediscounting facilities with the Central Bank. During 1974,
rediscounts nearly doubled in absolute terms, their share in the capital struc-
ture increasing from 11.6% in 1964 to 20.9% in 1973 and to 31.7% in 1974. Net
Central Bank support to TCZB jumped to TL 11,570 million in 1974 (Table 6,
Item 1.14).
49.       Deposits are still the major capital resource, though their share
in the balance sheet total dropped from 60.1% to 49.9% during 1974. The com-
position of the main deposit groups - time, savings, and current - remained
fairly stable, with time and savings constituting about two-thirds of the
total (Table 3, Item 5.06).
50.       Loans outstanding grew at a compound annual rate of nearly 22% dur-
ing the 11 years reviewed, the two last years showing accelerated increases
of 43% (1973) and 47% (1974), mainly due to the surge in lending to coopera-
tives. While the proportion of total assets represented by loans remained
around 70% over the period (Table 3, Item 9.01), the proportion of arrears to
total loans outstanding has ranged from a low of 10% in 1967 to a high of 25%
in 1972 (Table 3, Item 9.03). The relative decrease of overdue loans in 1973
and 1974 should be seen in the context of the tremendous increase in the lending
volume; there is no evidence of this development being the result of TCZB's
more effective collections performance.
51.       The question of overdue loans requires special attention because:
(a) outstanding loans affected by arrears are now equivalent to over
three times TCZB's equity;
(b) bad debt reserves of 0.2% of overdues seem inadequate (Table 6,
Item 3.03), especially since about 80% of the overdue loans by
end of 1974 had been classified as "under legal action" (there
is no data available on the ageing of arrears); and
(c) between 1969 and 1974 loans written off annually have averaged
less than 0.01% of overdue loans, which seems to be extremely low,
suggesting the need for a thorough scrutiny of TCZB's practice
in this respect.



ANNEX 2
Page 15
52.       Writing off bad bebts is seldom done, and only after a decision by
the Board of Directors. Write-offs in comparison to overdue loans developed
as follows:
