Only 7% of the amounts written off during the last six years were agricul-
tural loans, though such loans account for the vast majority of the loan
portfolio and of overdue loans. rhis underlines TCZB's optimistic view with
regard to recovering outstanding amounts from agricultural borrowers.
53.       The share of agricultural loans in the portfolio was considerably
increased during the period under review. Whereas agricultural loans (Table
3, Item 6.02) accounted for 66% of total loans not yet due (Table, Item 6.04)
at the end of 1964, the proportion was 87% at the close of 1974. Overdue ag-
ricultural loans accounted for 94% of amounts in arrears (Table 3, Item 6.05).
Liquid assets including statutory deposits remained between 27% and 35% of
total deposits throughout the years reviewed (Table 6, Item 2.05).
Income Statement
54.       In analyzing TCZB's income statements, it should be borne in mind
that provisions for bad and doubtful debts as well as write-offs do not seem
to correspond to requirements or to generally accepted norms. Therefore it
is difficult to express an opinion on the real profitability of TCZB. Nominal
profitability has been moderate during the period under review.
55.       TCZB's published accounts show profits before corporation tax.
Before-tax profits declined between 1966 and 1971, increased dramatically in
1972 and 1973, and decreased slightly in 1974 (Table 4, 4.01). The same trend
was exhibited in the relationship between profits and net worth (Table 6,
Item 4.02). With the exceptions of 16.7% and 14.5% before-tax returns on
equity realized in 1973 and 1974, respectively, profits remained at 9% (1972)
of net worth or below throughout the period and averaged 6.9%. The growth in
return on net worth in 1972 and 1973 as well as the relatively high return
rate in 1974 (amounting to more than twice the average of the 11 years under
review) reflect the absolute increase in interest and commissions earned (Table
4, Items 1.01 and 1.02) coupled with a declining wage bill relative to gross



Page 16
banking income (Table 6, Item 5.03). However, in 1974 this effect was partly
offset by a comparatively fast growth in interest costs (Table 4, Item 2..3),
which were increased by 62% compared to only a 40% increase in gross banking
income (Table 4, Item 1.06). Consequently, net banking income in relation to
total banking assets (Table 6, Item 4.17) was reduced in 1974.
56.       The relationship between the total wage bill and gross banking in-
come (Table 6, Item 5.03), slightly declining in 1972 and 1973 and considerab-
ly decreasing in 1974, is superficially commendable; however, management has
openly complained about the difficulty of holding good people in junior posi-
tions because of unattractive salary scales imposed on State Economic Entar-
prises. Perhaps more attractive salaries would reduce staff turnover in the
short run and increase institutional vitality in the long run.
57.       TCZB income from banking assets ranged from 7.1% to 8.8% over the
period (Table 6, Item 4.15), while the costs of banking assets remained be-
tween 3.1% and 5.0% (Table 6. Item 5.09). These costs include taxes and du-
ties paid (Table 6, Item 5.07), a major proportion of which is in respect of
the financial transactions tax, which may be conservatively considered a cost
of obtaining resources. Net banking income ranged from 3.1% to 4.1% of total
banking assets (Table 6, Item 4.17).
Sources and Uses of Funds
58.       The cash flow presentation in Table 5 has a format which is intend-
ed to reflect management priorities, suggesting that profits plus increases
in deposits and capital subscriptions are the primary resources at management's
disposal. Some of these resources are absorbed by statutory deposit require-
ments, by the accumulation of loans in arrears, by liquidity requirements, and
by retirement of bonds outstanding. The remainder is considered to be avail-
able for profitable lending and investing, and increases in lending create
a further possibility to obtain resources through use of rediscounting facili-
ties. Changes in other assets and liabilities as well as profits/losses of
previous years (see Table 5, footnote) and investments in fixed assets balance
out the funds available or required after lending and investment.
59.       The cash flow statement shows that management has had an increasing
amount of funds at its disposal (Item 2.03) and that the proportion of these
funds absorbed by statutory deposits (Item 3.02) and by increases in loan
arrears (Item 3.04) has varied greatly from year to year. Changes in liquid
assets (Items 4.01 and 4,02) have likewise fluctuated widely, leaving a highly
variable funds flow available for lending and equity investments (Item 5.01).
The volume of loans not yet due has increased by widely fluctuating amounts
(Item 6.01), and changes in the level of rediscounts (Item 6.02) have likewise
been erratic. Equity investments (Item 6.05) have increased only marginally,
since during most of Lhe period TCZB has been prohibited from making such
investments except to increase the capital of enterprises in which it is al-
ready a shareholder.



ANNEX 2
Page 17
E. Needs for Strengthening TCZB
60.       The need for a thorough review of TCZB's organization, policies,
olperations and finances has been recognized. As indicated in the main text,
t1he TCZB Management will undertake studies in order to prepare a program of
improvement. The terms of reference for the studies will be developed in due
course. While in no way anticipating the scope of these studies, the follow-
ing is an indication of the main areas of improvement as assessed during ap-
praisal (they are summarized in the text).
61.       Strengthening Structure.  Appropriate changes in TCZB's organization
and administrative procedures should be directed to improving its effectiveness
as a development institution and to enable it to cope with expanding activities.
Proven organizational principles, such as delegation, clarity and simplicity,
should guide the restructuring of its head office. Logically related functions
should be united under one supervisor.
62.       Loan approval authority should be decentralized.  In particular,
'the requirement that loans exceeding TL 150,000 need Board approval should be
revised. This would speed up loan process4ng. It would also allow the Board
to devote more time to policy matters.
63.       Accounting Principles and Precedures.  Review- of these areas should
lead to the establishment of divergences between TCZB's accounting practices
and generally accepted accounting ?rinciples, and proposals for short run and
long term changes, within the Turkish system. Particular efforts should be
made to introducing proper portfolio evaluatior., valuation of overdues and
write-off policy.
64.       TCZB should make better use of its accounting staff, of the existing
technical facilities, and of the possibilities offered by modern data proces-
sing. Computerized accounting should provide:
-    detailed information on the loar portfolio3 especially a
breakdown by age of loans overdue:
-    an early warning system for reminding borrowers of their
repavment obligations and for systematic loan recovervy
-    timely financial statements and other essential information
as required for management nurDoses.
65.       Internal Auditinz.  TCZB's Board of InsDectors should be strengthen-
ed to intensify and expand internal auditinQ.  The routine audits of the
branches should be revised with a view to ohtaining detailed information on
overdues and collection procedures. ard steps taken to remind delinquent bor-
rowers.  Each branch should he audited once a year.



ANNEX 2
Page 18
66,       Improved Resource Mobilization.  The need for TCZB increasing its
equity base is discussedl in the main text. TCZB should explore possibilities
to use branches to mobilize rural private savings; and its client credit co-
operatives as a base for expanding banking services in rural areas. This
would require adequate training of the cooperatives' staff in procedures
relating to savings accounts and other banking transactions. TCZB should also
try to increase its deposit relationships with borrowers.
67.       Project Appraisal.  TCZB's appraisal capacity needs strengthening,
in view of present weaknesses and expanding lending activities. The applica-
tion of modern appraisal techniques should ensure that:
(a) Projects financed by TCZB are technically feasible, financially
viable, commercially sound and economically justified, and
markets and processing facilities for their incremental pro-
duction are available;
(b) Projects will generate sufficient cash flow to cover debt
service, taxes and reinvestment needs and achieve adequate
financial rates of return; when determining the loan amount,
due consideration should be given to the financial require-
ments, considering investments in fixed as well as in current
assets.  Repayment schedules should be based on realistic
estimates of project cash flows and borrower's repayment capa-
cities. TCZB should shift from the system of declining an-
nuities to equal annuities.
(c) Borrowers have a satisfactory financial condition and structure.
68.       To ensure uniform appraisal criteria and reduce arbitrariness of
field staff, TCZB should introduce standard procedures and norms for:
-    farmers' or other subborrowers' contribution towards investment
cost taking into account differing down payment capacities of
small and big farmers;
-    repayment schedules, based on cash-flow projections that
take into account farmers' need to use a portion of the
incremental income for basic necessities; such a method
would give longer loan repayment periods to smaller farmers;
-    investment costs, crop pattern, yields, production cost,
incomes and repayment capacity by region.



ANNEX 2
Page 19
69.       Loan Arrears and Collection.  The need for studying and remedying
TCZB's seriouis arrearages situation has been discussed. TCZB should introduce
a loan supervision system and more effective collection procedures to improve
resource mobilization 1/ and borrowers' credit discipline. The information
system should provide a breakdown of arrears by age, separately for principal
and interest, as of December 31 of every year, starting with 1975.
1/   A reduction of the amounts overdue as of December 31, 1974 (TL 5.4 bil-
lion) by only 5% would mobilize TL 270 million, equal to TCZB's increase
in net worth (including profit) during the years 1968-1974.

APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT AND AGROINDUSTRIES PROJECT
TURKEY
The Supervised Credit Pogram Component (SCP) 1/
I. The Existing Program
A. Historical Background
1.        The Agricultural Bank of Turkey (TCZB) has, over the years. conducted
research to improve its agricultural credit services. A study made from 1961
to 1963 in collaboration with USAID recommended that a supervised credit pro-
gram be established, based on the Farmers Home Administration (FHO) model in
the U.S.A. 2/ The program was begun in 1964 involving the selection of two
pilot provinces with the intention of expanding the program throughout Turkey.
The program has cumulatively covered 34,000 farmers (slightly more than 1% of
the national total) in 50 of the cotmtry's 67 provinces, with coverage expected
to expand further to all provinces and TCZB branches within the next five years.
2.        Although referred to as supervised credit in English documents and
conversations and modelled on the FHA form, the Turkish program varies in
substance from the conventional concept of supervised credit. The program is
in fact an adaptation to local conditions and may be defended as such. The
shortage of funds (Annex 2, para 35) require that agricultural credit be
severely rationed, and such rationing is manifested in TCZB's ordinary agri-
cultural credit limits, expressed in fixed amounts per decare of cropped area,
which amount to a small proportion of the cash costs of growing the crop in
question. Supervised credit is a way around these limits. Rationing of super-
vised credit is accomplished by restricting the number of borrowers rather than
the credit provided to each borrower.
1/   A detailed description of the Supervised Credit Program is found in
Thomas Stickley and Suha Satana, {'Small Farmer Credit in Turkey: The
Supervised Credit Program of the Turkish Republican Agricultural Bank,"
Small Farmer Credit in West Asia, Vol. ix, USAID Spring Review of Small
Farmer Credit, Washington, Februarv 1973, pp 1-66.  Discussions with TCZB
officials suggested that the material in the Spring Review is reasonably
accurate, containing no material errors or misinterpretations.
2/   An initial fund of TL 50 million ($3.6 million) was established, half
of this amount beinz subscribed by TJSAID.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
B. General Objectives
3.        The objectives of the SCP are to:
(a)  increase productivitv per unit of land-,
(b) promote the use of improved and appropriate production inputs
and encourage on-farm investments;
(c)  help individual farmers operate economically:
(d) create an effective farmer demand for new agricultural
technology: and, in order to achieve these:
(e) provide the farmers with financial support in the form of
effective and adequate credit if the purchase of capital
goods and inputs lies beyond their capabilities.
T'echnical assistance by TCZB field staff represents a major program input.
UJnder the program, participating farmers are expected to achieve:
(a)  an improved and reasonable level of living-
(b) an ability to repay the loan;
(c) financial strength in being self-supporting (and graduating
from the program); and
(d) sufficient income to enjoy profits over the costs of
production.
C. Terms of Loans
4r.       SCP loans are advanced for operating expenses and investment.  Loans
are classified as short-term (loans of not more than one year); medium-term
('1-5 year loans for animal husbandry and agricultural imachinery); and long-term
(loans longer than 5 years for land development and improvement, irrigation
development, and on-farm building construction). The bulk of SCP loans are
griven for medium-term purposes, 6% for long-term and 25% for short-term pur-
poses. The distribution of cumulative disbursements from 1964 to the end of
11974 given below provides an indication of the general form and scope of the
ISCP .

D. SCP Credit Standards
5.        An applicant must fulfill the following requirements in order to be
eligible for a TCZB-SCP loan. He must:
(a)  be registered in the village or ward bureau of the census;
(b)  be a Turkish citizen;
(c)  be over 18 years old and in possession of his mental faculties,
(d)  be engaged in farming or fishing:
(e) not be excessively in debt to TCZB or other parties and be
capable of paying his debts (excluding debts deferred because
of natural calamities); (Those who otherwise fail to pay their
debts when due are not given further loans);
(f) not be a usureri
(g) request the credit for use in agricultural activities-,
(h) not be a member of the Bank's Board of Directors, the Bank's
staff or someone associated with them in a bond of interest:
(i) be a full-time agriculturalist, with no more than minor income
from sources other than the holding he works (not rigidly ad-
hered to);
(j)  be judged capable of adopting improved practices;
(k) be experienced and well-established in agriculture: and
(1) be able to satisfv collateral require.ments (see para 11).

In addition to being eligible for supervised credit, the farmers' land must
meet the following size limits wiich are specified by climatic zone and irri-
gation status.

6.        A reference to the dist:ribution of private agricultural holdings by
size (Annex 1, para 4) suggests that probably 15-20% of the country's smallest
operators and 1-2% of the largest operators are also excluded from the SCP.
Small operators are excluded because of the questionable economic viability
of their farm business plus the relatively high administrative costs of very
small loans. However, the exclusion based on land area is not as limiting as
it may appear since many operators of micro-plots are not full-time farmers,
and hence are not within the scope of SCP in any case.
7.        Each beneficiarv must observe the specifications indicated in his
farm production and investment plan which is made before loans are extended.
Specifications are made oll Lhe basis of the capabilities of the farmer con-
cerned as well as the agricultural potential of the area. Generally, these
plans cover 5 years and include both seasonal production and investment loans.
Upon completion of the first 5-y,ear plan, a second 5-year extension is permitted.
Policy and Control
8.        The SCP operates under a Policv Committee which was formed in 1963
consisting, at the highest level, of representatives of the Ministries of
Commerce, Finance and Agriculture, the State Planning Office: and TCZB. A
USAID representative was originally a member. The Committee is a policy-
making mechanism to guide SCP which is then administered by the Supervised
Credit Division at TCZB Head Office (see Chart 1).
9.        Borrowers are selected from applicants by a Selection Committee
established for each county included in the program. The Committee consists
of the TCZB Branch manager, the respective managers of the area Agricultural
Credit Cooperatives (if the applicant is a member of the cooperative), local
officials of the Veterinary and Agriculture departments, a senior village
representative, an agricultural chamber representative. and the agricultural
engineer or technician of the local TCZB branch office (who has no vote).



ANNEX 3
Page 5
Leading Policies and Procedures
(a) Interest Rate
10.       The interest rate currently charged on all agricultural loans, includ-
ing supervised loans, is 117 including 0.5% commission fee. Before October,
1974 it was 9.5%.
(b)  Collateral
11.       The following forms of collateral are accepted for supervised loans:
(i)  Basic collaterals:  Land mortgage and joint liability (up to
TL 75,000 possible without mortgage of land).
(ii) Complementary collaterals (to bridge the gap): mortgage of
other immovables.
(iii)  Extra securities:  pledge of movables (animals, agricultural
machinery, etc.).
If no mortgageable collateral exists which the farmer himself can offer, a
maximum of TL 75,000 can be advanced for the entire planned period by present-
ing signatures of at least three farmers for joint liability. Borrowers may
also bridge their mortgageable collateral deficit by presenting acceptable and
mortgageable immovables owned by other people. The value of agricultural
land to serve as collateral is determined by a scale of land assessment (sub-
ject to periodic revision) or by an assessment committee consisting of the
local TCZB branch manager and agricultural engineer or technician.
Delegation of Loan Approval Authorty
12.       Managers of second class branches may approve SCP loans up to
TL 50,000; first class branch managers TL 75,000- and provincial central branch
managers TL 100,000. Amounts not exceeding TL 150,000 are within the authority
of the Division Chief at headquarters. Loans from TL 150,000 to TL 500,000 1/
require the approval of the Board of Directors.
The_Sup~ervis ion Element
13.       Supervision is provided bv agricultural engineers (degree holders)
and technicians (high school graduates) assigned to branches. As of December
31, 1974, 230 supervisors were located in the field, serving the 310 TCZB
branches with SCP loans outstanding to farmers. Supervisors engaged in pro--
motional activities in rulral meetine places such as coffee houses and mosques
and farmers are urged to apply for funds at the local TCZB branch. During
the first farm visit the supervisor assists the farmer with the application
forms, or verifies the details in the forms if the farmer has completed the
forms himself. The supervisor also ascertains whether the farmer meets eligi-
bilitv requirements which applv to his farm bulsiness and character. The gen--
eral feasibility of the loan request is also examined.
1/   The ceiling was raised from TL 150,000 to TL 500,000 in 1974.



ANNEX 3
Page 6
14.       In the next stage the branch office insures that the applicant ful--
fills the remaining eligibility criteria.  Once these details are completed,
which should not require more than one month, the supervisor again visits the
farm for the purpose of project design. A land-use plan is drawn up to cover
a three to five-year period. From the plan, and from a survey of the farmer's
wealth and income, credit needs are ascertained. A detailed annual plan is
made, and the loan application an,l related documents are submitted to the
proper level of authority within rCZB for approval, modification or rejection.
Poor performers are ejected from the program in cases of blatant failure to
observe the farm plan specificatiDns or the advice given by the Bank's tech-
nical staff,  Such farmers are given a 1-month notice 1/ and if no investment
occurs, they are expelled from the program and are required to repay outstand-
ing loans within a short time.
15.       Field supervision primarily involves frequent but short farmer visits
and good supervisors visit each farm once a month, although the effectiveness
of these visits mav be diminished by the severe fragmentation of many holdings
and hence the diminished probability of finding the operator on the plot chosen
for a visit. Many of these visits are not scheduled, although farmers specifi-
cally requesting a supervisory visit are given scheduled times. A more formal
review of the farmer's progress is made annually when he makes his request for
seasonal operating funds under the long range plan.
16.       TCZB policy provides that supervisors should spend no more than one
day a week in their offices, and this day is frequently chosen to coincide with
market day when farmers come to the local center. However, lack of adequate
transnort facilities (such as a branch Land Rover) often results in super-
visors remaining in their offices more than one dav a week.
17.       It is a moot Doint whether the supervisory input is more useful in
helping TCZB lend or the farmer borrow. Supervised credit is a means of pro--
moting agricultural progress in a more dynamic wav than available through
ordinarv TCZB agricultural credit, which is rationing tnder a procedure pro-
viding small amounts of credit to a large number of farmers. This rationing
procedure precludes a dynamic impact, while supervised credit can be concen-
trated in larger amounts. Supervision also reduces the Bank's risk in lending.
Collections
(a) Timing
18.       Collection of annual operational loans is carried out after the
harvestin2 season. Investment lcan installments are expected to be collected
at the same time. Supervised loans generally involve no security in the form
of Bank liens on crop deliveries or other forms of TCZB control over borrowers'
cash inflows.
1/   SCP Management is considering a change of the 1-month notice:  A
reasonable period of time', adequate to the circurstances, may be
introduced instead of the 1-month deadline.



ANNEX 3
Page 7
(b) Repayment Performar.ce
19.       The amount of loans not collected as a percent of the amount of
loans that should have been collected decreased from 47% in 1970 to 34%, 28.5%,
28.4% and 24.2% in 1971, 1972, 1973 and 1974 respectively. Up to 1973 there
was a similar improvement in the number of delinquent borrowers: Their per-
centage dropped from 23.8% in 1970 to 19.7%O, 14.6% and 14.2% in 1971, 1972 and
1973 respectively. However, in 1974, this percentage increased sharply to
26.1% suggesting that in this year repayment performance of the small borrowers
deteriorated.
(c) Rescheduling
20.       Debts may be rescheduled if justification offered for failure to
repay is approved by the branch concerned. Eligible reasons may be natural
disasters or other factors beyond the farmer's control. In such cases, debts
may be repaid in installments or all at once in the following cropping season.
E. Farm Planning and Subloan Appraisal
21.       The appraisal and scrutiny of each loan application for supervised
credit is an elaborate process. The considerable detail of data obtained on
the individual from history and future plan results in a substantial base for
appraisal, viz., determining the financial feasibility of the proposed in-
vestments in terms of incremental benefits and costs and arranging credit as
well as repayment of loan as indicated by the cash flows.
F. Analvsis of SCP Performance
22.       Tables 1-5 in this Annex give a provisional analysis of TCZB's
performance under the Supervised Credit Program. The analysis is provisional
because it incorporates numerous assumptions to make up for the lack of hard
data as detailed in table footnotes. This deficiency reflects TCZB's organi-
zational structure and approach to decision-making. The management of the
Supervised Credit Division, for example, should have more authority and con-
trol over such matters as staffing, promotions and remuneration, vehicle ac-
quisition, etc. TCZB management appears to evaluate SCP on the basis of the
cumulative number of loans sanctioned since the program's inception, with less
attention to the annual volume of approvals; on the number of Provinces covered
by the program: and by the proportion of total loans outstanding which are in
arrears. Borrowers' performance is measured largely by the proportion of on-
farm investment, plans fulfilled, and increases in their net worth.



ANNEX 3
Page 8
S'CP Coverga
23.       The number of SCP borrowers has increased in each of the years since
1969 and that the number of farmers in the program as of end-1974 approximated
22,400 (Table 1). About 30% of all farmers who have received SCP loans since
ithe program's inception in 1964 have graduated from the program, and field
research data undertaken by management indicates that borrower net worth has
increased in each of these cases. Statistics collected on 2,150 graduated
farmers showed that their net worth increased by 24.67 or an average of TL
19,505. The average rate of realization of planned investments for these
farmers was 89.5%.  Amone the farmers surveyed, the highest increase of net
worth was TL 559,700.
24.       The volume of loans sanctioned under the program has increased sub-
stantially since the end of 1971 and that the average loan approved approxi-
matelv doubled between 1969-71 andl 1972-74 (Table 1). Management explains
this change as reflecting an increase in emphasis on supervised credit and
the communication of a better understanding of program goals to field staff
and also to branch managers who are the officials who approve most SCP loans.
The increase in average loan size occurred in both the investment loan and
the operating loan categories, and the ratio between the two categories
appears to have altered from roughly 3:1 to 4:1 with the quantum change in
loan size which occurred in 1972.
Portfolio Size and Arrears
25.       While the portfolio more than tripled in size between 1969 and 1974,
arrears not even doubled (Table 1). In relative terms, while 27% of the port-
folio were overdue in 1969, this percentage declined to 15% in 1974.
Staffin  and Surpe _sory Inputs
26.       Staffing patterns have been somewhat erratic. although the general
trend has been towards more supervisors in the field as the program has ex-
panded (Table 2). The composition of the field staff has altered radically
between the two grades: engineers (degree holders) and technicians (secondarv
school graduates). While engineers outnumbered technicians by approximately
2 to 1 in 1969. almost the reverse situation prevailed at the end of 1974.
This downgrading of supervisory inputs in terms of paper qualifications ap-
pears not to have adversely affected portfolio performance (indeed, portfolio
performance has improved), and also has not been accompanied bv a decline in
staff productivity as measured by indicators discussed below. Obviously, some
engineers are required under the present system since they are the only super-
visors eligible for eventual elevation to managerial positions under TCZB's
civil-service-tvpe regulations. It may well be that a continued decrease in
the proportion of cadre consisting of engineers would not affect the perform-
ance of SCP adversely.



ANNEX 3
Page 9
27.       The average supervisorv case loads have ranged from 82 to 93 farmers
during the period under review (Table 3, section II). Much of this increase
orobably reflects TCZB's difficulties in holding qualified supervisors rather
than productivitv increases obtained by other means. Salaries are not attrac-
tive relative to those available in the private sector and turnover in the
cadre has probably been massive, although management compiles no turnover
data. TCZB, as a State Economic Enterprise, is able to secure a better deal
for its staff only after remuneration levels have risen outside the public
sector, and therefore is always one square behind in this particular game.
When inflation accelerates, TCZB's relative position is eroded more rapidly,
as bureaucratic response time is more or less a constant, The hypothesis that
staff departures have been a major factor increasing case loads is supported
by the decline in the proportion and more recently in the number of engineers
assigned to SCP field cadre as mentioned above. Engineers, because of their
superior paper qualifications, have more job options than technicians, and
therefore, other things being equal, it is no doubt more difficult to hold
good engineers than to hold good technicians.
28.       Loan volume per supervisor, as shown by Table 3, has increased from
TL 1.38 million (1969) to TL 2.08 million (1974), average volume sanctioned
grew from TL 0.99 million (1969) to TL 1.22 million (1974).
SCP Costs
29.       An attempt has been made to estimate the costs of supervisory staff
and to identify and quantify other SCP costs and overheads relative to the
program's demands upon TCZB resources and services as a whole (Tables 4 and 5).
Total program costs estimated in this manner have more than tripled over the
period under review, from TL 19 million in 1969 to TL 64 million in 1974.
While the total costs per account per year have increased from around TL 1,750
in 1969-70 to TL 2,850 in 1974, they have remained relatively stable as a pro-
portion of SCP loan balances outstanding, ranging from 13% to 16% and trending
downwards with the expansion in the number and average size of loan accounts
which occurred with the rise in 1973.
30.       Given the current lending rate of 10.5% on SCP loans and the 0.5%
annual commission charge, and the optimistic assumption that the supervisory
inputs provided to borrowers have created increases in borrower output and
resource productivitv which could not otherwise have been realized, the cost
of the program does not appear excessive and program administration and control
appear quite effective. The major task of management under expansion of the
program would be to maintain the favorable lender's performance realized to
date and to use new information to rationalize operations and to free re-
sources for the provision of supervised credit to more farmers, with special
attention to the smaller onerators who have not received a great deal of
emphasis under the program to date.



ANNErX 3
Page 10
II.  The Project's Contribution
A.  Streajthenin _of the Existing Program
31.       Under the proposed project, additional funds and suDporting services
would be made available to strenmthen and broaden the existing, integrated farm
development Drogram. In addition to TCZB's present expansion plans, loans
amountinr to TL 280 million (USS20 million) would be made over the project
period tc reach about 4,000 farmers in 50 of the 67 provinces.
32.       Investments would be made in various fields with em.phasis on produc-
tion and on-farm storage of fruits and earlv vegetables, and livestock produc-
tion. Farm model analysis has been carried out for 8 representative models
covering about 70% of the investments (Tables 6-11). The projected numbers
and amounts of investments are shown in Annex 8.
33.       All prices, yields and other technical coefficients used in the
models are based on information received in May 1975. Although input and
output prices are likely to change with time, it is anticipated that they will
increase at the same rate. Therefore, in all the models constant prices have
been applied. Where applicable, i.e. in the construction of barns or orchards,
a physical contingency of 5% has been included in the investment costs.
34.       The financial rates of return for 8 representative farm models and
those from sensitivity tests are shown below:
Financial                                Gross
Rate of    Investment   Operating    Revenue
Farm Model                         Return      Costs + 10%  Costs + 10;  - 10%

35.       The farm model analvsis presented in Tables 6-12 would also assist
TCZB in revising its appraisal methods. With the assistance of the prepara-
tion and appraisal missions the SCP staff have already carried out farm model
analyses. It is planned to apply income and cash flow analysis to about 10%
of the farms receiving loans under the project and compute financial rates of
return in a detailed model analysis for each major investment category. Addi-
tional assistance would be given under the training program to be provided by
FAO (Annex 7, paras 11-16).



ANNEX 3
Page 11
B. Introduction of a Short-Term Program
36.       To broaden participation especially among smaller farmers and in the
less developed areas of central and eastern Anatolia, a new short-term lending
program would be introduced under the project. This program would provide the
participating farmers with sufficient amounts of short-term credit and tech-
nical advice to achieve a considerable increase in productivity. It thus com-
pares favorably with the short-term credit TCZB gives to farmers under its
general lending activities where farmers receive only small amounts (Annex 2,
para 37) and use, most likely, substantial portions of the loans for consump-
tion. The premise of the new program is to utilize capable manpower available
in the villages for technical supervision.
37.       Village agricultural extension agents would be appointed by the
Ministry of Agriculture under the program. They would closely cooperate with
experienced TCZB staff (engineers and technicians) and be responsible for:
(a) certifying the eligibility of farmers to receive such loans, and
(b) supervising the technical performance of the participating
farmers (e.g. by checking the status of the fields several times
a year).
38B       TCZB, in collaboration with the Ministry of Agriculture, would es-
tablish criteria for technical eligibility. After eliminating delinquent
farmers, TCZB would sanction loans in accoance with standard guidelines
for financing selected crop or livestock inputs to be agreed between TCZB
and the Ministry of Agriculture. To keep the program simple, thereby in-
creasing its effectiveness, each TCZB branch office would concentrate on
3 crop and/or livestock categories at most. Credits would be based on
relevant crop acreage and/or livestock numbers.
39.       The program would be initiated in two or three provinces, preferably
in less developed parts of the country. An estimate on the expected coverage
is given below:
Year
1              2                3
Incremental Total Incremental Total
Participating Branches              3            4        7         5        12
TCZB Engineers/Technicians          9            12       21       15        36
Village Agricultural Extension
Agents                           72           96      168       120       288
Participating Farmers           3,600        4,800    8,400    6,000    14,400



ANNEX 3
Page 12
40.       The estimates are based on the following assumptions:
(i)  An agent would be responsible for 1-2 villages involving
about 50 farmers receiving credit.
(ii)  A TCZB engineer/technician would work with 8 village agri-
cultural extension agents.
(iii)  A TCZB branch comprises about 80 villages.  Since the program
is intended to cover the whole area of each branch selected,
three TCZS engineers/'technicians would be needed per branch.
41.       Although TCZB has no experience with such a program as yet, this
project component seems to be promising in view of the success that has been
achieved with a similar approach in the Seyhan Irrigation Project. A farm
budget analysis has been determined for a representative farm model in central
Anatolia yielding satisfactory results (Table 13). Farmers would receive
100% of the incremental costs which are estimated at TL 3,650 per farm.  The
loans would be repaid after about 12 months and relent thereafter. At full
development, the program is estimated to reach about 14,400 farmers with loans
amounting to TL 67.2 million.

ANNEX 4

APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT AND AGROINDUSTRIES PROJECT
TURKEY
The Agroindustries Component
Background
1.        Food manufacturing in Turkey accounts for about one-third of the
total industrial production. The output of manufactured foods and beverages
increased by 70% between 1962 and 1972 and export earnings increased from
US$43 million in 1963 to US$87 million in 1972. The following table shows
the growth of the various sub-sectors:
Food Industries Growth Index (1972)
in Turkey
1962 = 100
Sea and Lake Produce                                  1,500
Tea Industry                                             472
Canning Industry                                         372
Sugar Industry                                           196
Milk and Milk Products                                   195
Vegetable Oil Industry                                   184
Confectionary Industry                                   169
Meat and Meat Products                                   165
Flour and Biscuit Industrv                               149
General Food Industry Index                              168
Source:  `Turkey Exports"s, Chambers of Industry and Commerce
(Istanbul), Table 1 gives production figures from
1970 to 1973 for selected commodities.
2.        Another measure of this growth is the credit demand for new agro-
industries. The outstanding credit balances of the Agricultural Bank of
Turkey (TCZB) for this type of projects were:
Year                            TL Million
1971                               23.0
1972                               55.0
1973                              134.0
1974                              300.0



ANNEX 4
Page 2
The availability of surplus agricultural raw materials, increasing domestic
and export demand, and various specific Government incentives are important
factors motivating both private and public sector investors to expand existing
capacity or to build new plants.
3.        The principal institutions responsible for the development of
agroindustries are:
(a) The State Planning Organization (SPO), which is in charge
of setting up goals and programs for the various sectors of
the economy and establishing the necessary incentives.
(b) The Ministry of Industries, which administers the granting
of incentives.
(c) The Ministry of Agriculture, which provides support and
technical assistance, through the Projects Unit (PU) and
several research institutions (Citrus, Olive, Canning,
Leather, Agricultural Research Institutes, etc.)
(d) The Directorate of Cooperatives of Ministry of Village Affairs
(DC-MVA) which is in charge of assisting and helping to or-
ganize cooperatives in the rural areas, for which some finan-
cial resources are provided in the form of grants and credits.
(e) TCZB which supplies the bulk of the Government credit
available for agroindustries.
(f) IGEME which is an export-promoting agency under the
Ministry of Commerce carries out research in the field of
marketing, assisting exporters and coordinating export
activities.
4.        Shortage of credit and lack of technical assistance have conspired
against the sector. Due to lack of financial resources, TCZB has been
forced to reject 40 to 50% of the loan applications and to grant inadequate
loan amounts for approved loan requests. There are other projects which did
not satisfy requirements because the entrepreneur (generally a cooperative
of farmers) did not have sufficient experience to present them in a form
suitable for financial review. In other instances, projects which were
initiated based on incomplete and out-of-date feasibility studies, realized
too late that either financial plans, technical and engineering aspects, raw
material availability, market outlets or other factors had not been taken
into account. Despite these negative aspects, agroindustries in Turkey offer
a good potential for investment and further development. Bank participation
would not only provide credit but would help strengthen existing institutions
and organize technical assistance to minimize failures and waste of resources.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
Objectives
5.        The proposed Agroindustrial Credit component would set the fol-
lowing objectives:
(a) Facilitate the establishment of new enterprises and help
expand, complete and vertically integrate existing installa-
tions for agroindustrial activities.
(b) Build on the capabilities of existing Turkish institutions,
especially of the Project Unit (PU) of the Ministry of Agri-
culture, TCZB and the DC-MVA. This would strengthen their
competence in project preparation and appraisal, through
training of professional staff, which in turn would provide
technical assistance and extension services to enterprises,
cooperatives or individuals.
(c) Reduce waste and spoilage of existing agricultural raw
materials through the construction of packing and storage
facilities and find new outlets, through processing into
higher price commodities, for the internal and external
markets.
(d) Support and complement other production-oriented investment
under this project (Fresh Fruit and Vegetable Exports and
the Livestock II) and other Bank-suggested projects (Annexes
3 and 5).
These objectives are in line with the development policies and goals of Gov-
ernment and of the institutions involved.
Eligible Project Investments
6.        The Agroindustries component would provide credit for:  (a) estab-
lishment of new facilities including initial working capital; (b) expansion,
modernization and vertical integration of existing plants and installations;
and (c) cooperative-type projects for village-based rural industry investment.
Loan applications may be divided in the following categories:
(a)  Domestic and export handling facilities, including grading
and packing installations for fruits, vegetables, eggs,
poultry, meat, etc.
(b) Cold storage facilities, generally integrated with packing
and/or processing plants, including those necessary for
slaughterhouses.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
(c)  Processin plants, using agricultural raw materials to
produce commodities with higher value-added components
which would be sold on export and local markets. Examples
of processing plants eligible for support under the project
are:
-    tomato paste; citrus frozen concertrates; fruit juice
concentrates (grape, cherry. peach, apricot, etc.);
extraction and refining of vegetable oils (olive,
sunflower, etc.):. slaughterhouses (cattle, sheep,
poultry); feed mills; alfalfa meal; canning of
vegetables and fruits (mushrooms included); potato
processing (chips, starch, granules, etc.); dehydration
of garlic and onions; fruit drying essential oils
(mint, aniseed, etc.); wineries; hatcheries; by-
product utilization, including the processing of
peels, cores, seeds, etc.
(d) Other facilities which would provide support, manufacture
inputs or facilitate farm production or materials for agro-
industries, such as:
-    simple agricultural machinery and equipment- card-
board box plants: can factories.
Seven models (tables 1-7) show examples of typical investments. They detail
assumptions, parameters and cash flow analysis, as a basis of subloan evalua-
tion. Other investments under the above four categories would also be elig-
ible for project support, Subloans would normally be limited to US$3 mill-
lion ($8 million total investment cost).
Responsibilities
7.        Two agencies would be involved in implementing the agroindustries
component: PU and TCZB. One of their functions would be to advise potential
subborrowers on engineering (technical feasibility), marketing and financial
planning as required by TCZB in support of subloan requests. PU is expected
to assume the greatest responsibility because of the projected strong demand
for marketing facilities and cold stores. Its demonstrated competence in
assisting subborrowers for marketing facilities under the Fresh Fruit and
Vegetable Export project would also be expanded to cover processing. TCZB
would provide technical assistance to the remaining eligible investors. The
second important function namely financial evaluation of subloan requests,
would be the sole responsibility of TCZB. Arrangements for staff training
and supporting services in respect of these two functions are discussed in
Annex 7. The DC-MYA would sponsor applications from cooperatives for invest-
ments in small village-based industries.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
Constraints
8.        For medium- and long-term loans TCZB requires semi-annual repayment
of principal with interest charged on the remaining balance. This leads to
high interest payments during the first years when revenues are just beginning
to build up. Sub-borrowers' repayment burden would be more in line with re-
payment capability and TCZB's prospects of timely loan collection would be
enhanced by introducing a debt-service structure based on equal annual install-
ments (Annex 2, para 40).
9.        Container costs are high in Turkey.  The use of large containers
(steel drums, etc.) is presently discouraged because of the high cost and the
policy of the government to grant an export rebate for containers of 5 kg
capacity or less. The tendency in other countries is to simplify handling
and reduce packing and transport costs by using large size containers. Large
containers, however, require special cleaning and filling equipment and more
careful processing techniques to assure good quality.
10.       The information on export and local markets is still incomplete and
inconsistent. For foreign markets, information on quality standards, import
duties, freight costs, etc., are available primarily to a few exporting
companies with several years of experience in foreign trade. To assist in
subproject marketing analysis, both domestic and export, provisions have been
provided as part of the technical assistance component of the project for a
marketing specialist for 12 months.
Model Assumptions
11.       The seven illustrative models represent typical subprojects.  Where
available, on-site information was used to develop project parameters. Some
costs of services were taken as a percentage of the fixed investment excluding
land (maintenance and repair and insurance) or of total sales (sales expenses),
at levels considered appropriate in Turkey.
12.       For certain export items (tomato paste, and fruit and citrus juice
concentrate,) an export rebate has been granted by the Turkish Government.
However, there are indications that these may be eliminated (for tomato paste)
or substantially reduced (for other juice concentrates). Model assumptions
reflect this possibility.



ANNEX 4
Page 6
13.       Of the total investment costs, 70% including the initial working
capital, would be financed by long-term credit and the remaining 30% would be
owners' contribution. Additional seasonal credit (short-term) needed after
the first year of operation would be obtained from TCZB or from commercial
banks. TCZB would satisfy itself that provisions for seasonal working capital
are adequate.
Representative Models
14.       Tomato paste.  Turkey has favorable climatic and soil conditions for
tomato production, and with organization and management, tomatoes for process-
ing could be obtained at reasonable prices (TL 0.5/kg. including transport to
the factory). In order to assure the steady supply of tomatoes, the enter-
prise would sign contracts with the farmers and provide the seedlings of early,
normal and late varieties in quantities that would meet the production program
requirements. Investments to achieve this end are included in the model
(Table 2). In recent years production of tomatoes in Turkey has been increas-
ing as shown below:
Year                       Million Tons
1970                            1.8
1971                            1.9
1972                            2.0
1973                            2.1
Source:   State Institute of Statistics.
15.       Present licensed capacity for tomato paste is about 62,000 tons per
year, however, 60 to 70% of the installed capacitv utilizes the batch process-
ing system, generally inadequate for the high qualitv requirements of external
markets. There are also an unknown number of firms which obtained a license,
but to date have not started production.
16.       Internal markets for tomato paste have been growing, the demand
coming mainly from fisheries, prepared meals factories, restaurants, hotels,
etc. In 1974, the internal demand consumed about 20,000 tons or 1/3 of the
output. This figure does not include the homemade product which is still the
major source for the Turkish families. The potential market in Arab countries
is important. According to a study being prepared by the International Trade
Center, about 2/3 of the estimated 35,000 tons of tomato products consumed in
these countries in 1974, was imported. Here, demand appears to be for small
capacity containers (70 g. to 500 g. cans). For example. Lebanon has been
very successful in repacking imported tomato paste into smaller cans and sell-
ing it in other Arab countries. Two plants with a capacity to process 20 tons
per hour of tomatoes would be expected to be financed under the project. Pro-
duction would be about 50% for export and 50% for internal market.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
17.       Citrus frozen concentrate.  Production of citrus fruits in Turkey
in recent years is shown below:
1970    1971    1972    1973
----     '000 tons     
Oranges                          445      460     467      470
Lemons                           126      142     149      122
Grapefruit                         7        7       6      N.A.
Mandarin                          68       75      97       84
Source:   State Institute of Statistics.
The current production of citrus concentrate is mostly of the canned hot-pack
type and is consumed locally. Present licensed capacity is about 20,000 tons
of concentrate (mainly from oranges) but only 1/3 to 1/2 of the capacity is
in operating condition. One new plant under construction in Antalya will
produce frozen citrus concentrates.
18.       The main markets for the frozen concentrates (especially orange and
grapefruit) are the EEC countries. The use of large containers (55 gallon
steel drums) is a way of reducing packaging, handling and transportation costs.
However, the Government of Turkey grants the export rebate only to containers
of 5 kg or less which are difficult to sell in the EEC countries. Competition
is justified only when quality and prices are competitive. Since consumption
of soft drinks, beverages and fruit juices is expected to increase, market
prospects appear favorable in the Arab countries.
19.       Because of the uncertain market prospects it is expected that only
one plant with a capacity of 10 tons per hour of oranges would be financed
under the project (Table 2). Output from the plant would be primarily for
export.
20.       Grape juice concentrate.  With specific modifications in the ex-
traction process, the proposed plant (Table 3) could produce juice from other
fruits like apples, apricots and peaches. The production of these fruits in
Turkey has been as follows:
1970    1971    1972    1973
'000 tons             -----
Grapes                     3,680   3,853   3,434  3,344
Apples                       748      780     850    850
Peaches                      112       84     140   N.A.
Apricots                      52       34      63      77
Wild Apricots                 43       46      60   N.A.
Source:   State Institute of Statistics.



ANNEX 4
Page 8
Licensed capacity for multipurpose fruit juice concentrate plants is about
60,000 tons of concentrate per year. Again, several licensed plants have
never operated and others operate below installed capacity. Their products
are sold mostly on the local market, as raw materials for the preparation of
soft drinks, beverages, etc., although some exports have been made to Europe
and Arab countries. Total fruit juice exports from Turkey are shown below.
Exports of Fruit Juice Concentrates from Turkey
1972     1973      1974
Tons                                  285       471       325
TL ('0OOs)                          1,928    6,560    5,550
TL per ton                          6,765   13,928   17,077
US$ per ton                           483       995    1,220
Source:   State Institute of Statistics.
Several juice plants have been established by groups of farmers, merchants
and Turkish laborers and there is a strong credit demand for additional plants.
Raw materials (especially grapes and apples), usually from lower grade fruit,
are readily available at reasonable prices. Market demand for juice concen-
trates as an ingredient for soft drinks is expanding both domestically and
in the Arab countries.
21.       It is expected that at least one plant of this type will be financed
under this project. Some applications for the completion of vertical integra-
tion of existing plants would also be considered.
22.       Cold storageand_packing houses.  In order to take full advantage
of agricultural production, it is necessary to extend the marketing season of
some fruits and vegetables by storing them under controlled conditions. For
export markets and also for internal consumption, proper handling, storage,
cleaning, grading and packing of produce are important for higher returns.
All these operations contribute to better presentation and preservation of
quality for an extended period of time. Therefore, it is essential for both
exporters and growers to have installations of the types described above.
The same applies for processing plants to increase the utilization of their
capacity, operating over a larger number of days than the normal season would
allow.
23.       The increasing demand for apples, potatoes, dairy products, fresh
fruits and vegetables in European and Arab countries can only be met if enough
packing houses and cold stage units are built in the production areas. The
experience of Turkish farmers, cooperatives and exporters in this field is
growing, and there is considerable demand for credit. These cold storage and
packing houses would either sell their service to growers or exporters at a
fixed price per ton or else they would grow or buy and export on their own



ANNEX 4
Page 9
account. Under the first arrangement, boxes, bags, labels, etc., with dis-
tinctive trademarks and names are generaly provided by the customer, reducing
the need for working capital and space for inventories.
24.       It is assumed that at least 7 integrated facilities (packing house
and cold storage) would be financed (Table 4). Farmers' cooperatives includ-
ing those in the country's less developed regions are especially interested
in obtaining subloans for these facilities.
25.       Olive Oil.  Olive production in Turkey is as follows:
Year                  1,000 Tons
1970                       681
1971                       326
1972                     1,019
1973                       332
The low figures in 1971/1973 are due to cyclical phenomenon which results in
low yield every two to three years. The best table oil is extracted from
olives by the pressing method, the resultant crude oil being refined and
canned. Additional amounts of oil can be recovered from the press cake by
the solvent extraction method, which produces non-edible oil for industrial
purposes and requires large capacities to be conmercially feasible. There is
substantial demand for sma'll press-extraction units in the olive producing
areas. The crude oil to be sold to larger central refineries for further pro-
cessing, and in some cases, press cake for solvent extraction of the remaining
oil. Olive oil has good acceptance in world markets and Turkey has been ex-
porting about 50% of its total production.
Turkeye- Exports of Olive Oil
1972         1973          1974
Tons                                       3,413       44,400       11,062
TL ('000s)                           37,435      634,953      209,694
Average TL per ton                   10,970       14,300       18,956
Average US$ per ton                     784        1,022        1,354
Source:         State Institute of Statistics.
26.       Refined olive oil commands a higher price (TL 28 to 30 per kg) than
can be attained on the export market (TL 19 per kg). Investment in olive oil
plants is actively promoted by DC-MVA among cooperatives of olive growers.
Provisions for four olive oil plants have been included under the project
(Table 5).



ANNEX 4
Page 10
27.       Slaughterhouses in Turkey are operated by the central government's
Meat and Fish Organization (MFO), municipalities, and by private sector firms.
MFO accounts for about 10% of the recorded slaughterings in the country and
sells dressed carcasses to the armed forces and other institutional consumers.
Currently, due to the shortage of meat for the domestic markets, exports are
prohibited (Annex 1). However, increased investments in livestock production
and fattening have the long term objectives of providing meat mainly for the
domestic market with possibly some export potential.
28.       Most existing slaughterhouses are old, unsanitary and operationally
inefficient and are in need of modernization or replacement. Few have chill
rooms and frozen storage, adequate facilities for animal dehiding and eviserat-
ing, provisions to handle meat throughout the process in a manner to assure
clean products and sufficient space. Some livestock producing areas (i.e.,
Amasya and Gasiantep) do not have slaughtering facilities. Large numbers of
livestock are produced in Eastern Turkey far from population centers. Trans-
port charges plus weight losses during shipment for live animals increase the
cost of meat significantly. Increasing the weight of the cattle through
fattening programs would result in higher yields and better price competitive-
ness (Annex 5).
29.       Provisions for a minimum of two slaughterhouses have been included
in the project in areas where livestock production and fattening programs are
being developed. Each plant would have a daily killing capacity of about 100
cattle and 700 sheep. Design would include on-the-rail dressing, sanitary
construction standards, refrigerated storage and ancillary facilities (Table
6).
30.       Feed Mills.  At present, there are 68 feed mills operating in the
country with more planned by Government to assure the availability of feeds
in all regions. In 1972, feed production was 258,000 tons and increased to
280,000 tons in 1973. Poultry feed represented about 53% of the total output
with the balance being produced for livestock feed. In the past, cattle feed
which has been high-priced compared to carcass yield and selling price, has
been a small volume item. One low cost cattle feed ingredient, wet sugar beet
pulp, has been obtained from sugar processing factories and fed directly as a
dietary energy source. Beet pulp occasionally is dried and then incorporated
into mixed feeds. By-products from agri-industries processing are utilized on
a limited basis depending primarily on seasonal availability. Mixed feeds
are blended and balanced to provide for more predictable rates of gain from
livestock and poultry. Feed mix ingredients, grains, oilseed press cake,
and other protein concentrates, minerals and vitamins are in part available
domestically with the balance being imported.
31.       Feed prices used in the model (Table 7) reflect the current price
of ingredients in Turkey. Anticipated price decreases in the world grain
market would reduce mixed feed costs making mixed feed more attractive to
livestock and poultry farmers. In the project, provisions have been made
for at least one 10 ton per hour capacity feed mill to be located in the
Amasya area near sugar beet processing plants and livestock fattening farms.

ANNEX 5
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT AND AGROINDUSTRIES PROJECT
TURKEY
Cattle Fattening Component
A. Background
Need for Animal Fattening
1.        Meat consumption in Turkey has been increasing rapidly in recent
years due to a high income elasticity of demand coupled with high population
growth rates and urbanisation (apparent per capita beef consumption in 1973
was 5.3 kg). However the rate of growth of meat production has not kept pace
with the demand. This has resulted in Government intervention in the meat
market through minimum support prices for producers and retail price controls
for the benefit of consumers. The Third Plan (1973-77 inclusive) also reflects
Government desire to accelerate production and reduce exports of meat and other
livestock products in order to satisfy rising domestic demand (Annex 1).
Government's Choice of Agency
2.        It was against this background that the Turkish Planning Organisa-
tion entrusted the Turkish Sugar Factories Corporation (TSF) 1/ with a program
of cattle breeding, distribution of breeding stock to farmers and cattle fat-
tening in 1969. The choice of TSF as executing agency was primarily two-fold
(a) they had an existing widespread network of supervisors and contacts with
the farming community who grew sugar beet; and (b) the main by-products of
sugar extraction, namely beet pulp and molasses could form important ingre-
dients in cattle feed.
3.        The cattle fattening component of the TSF program is confined to
the sugar beet growers with whom TSF annually contracts for delivery of sugar
1/   TSF is a state economic enterprise established in 1935.  The corporation
comprises 17 factories of which 5 are classified as private but in these
latter TSF is the majority shareholder and provides all the organisation.
TSF is involved in a wide range of other activities including engineering
plants, alcohol production and stockholding interests in the Sugar Bank,
Sugar Insurance Company, Seed, Improvement and Marketing Corporation and
a Nitrogen Plant. In 1975 TSF plans an expansion program of TL 814 mil-
lion in its sectors of operation of food, agriculture and machinery.



ANNEX 5
Page 2
beet.  Sugar beet is grown by most farmers under a four year rotation with
cereals, alfalfa and/or sunflower or other crops. Only farmers with 100
decares or more of irrigable land are allowed to grow sugar beet annually,
areas with less than this amount must grow the crop once in 4 years. The total
number of sugar beet growers in the country is 450,000 but on average only
250,000 are awarded contracts annually. In 1974, the 250,000 growers, with
a total of 170,000 hectares planted to sugar beet, lived in 7,000 villages
distributed over 51 provinces. These villages were assisted by a TSF tech-
nical staff of 650, comprising 200 district agents and 450 subordinates.
Existing TSF Program
4.        Objectives:  The principal aims of the program are several:
(a) To diversify the income pattern of beet growers by providing
financial resources and employment during the winter months.
(b) To improve the yield of meat from livestock. This is done
by removing from the land cattle which are otherwise over-
stocked and undernourished, particularly in the Eastern Prov-
inces. These vould otherwise either be slaughtered due to
lack of winter feed or be retained for several additional
years growing at rates-little above maintenance.
(c) To increase the animal population of beet farmers and
thereby the availability of farmyard manure.
(d) To encourage the production of forage crops such as alfalfa.
These provide direct constituents to the ration of cattle
plus filling an important position in the crop rotation of
sugar beet production.
5.        Procedure:  Contracts between sugar beet growers and TSF are drawn
up during the winter months for the beet crops of the following season. These
farmers are asked to join the fattening program in groups of at least 5 and
no more than 20. Each group of farmers has a leader and deputy who are the
main points of contact with TSF. All signatories of the group are equally
liable for repayment of loans in the case of default of one of their members.
Each member of the group should be willing to fatten no fewer than 3 and no
more than 40 head.
6.        Credit is made available to members of the eligible groups to cover
up to 70% of the purchase cost of the animals and feed costs over the fattening
period. The income from the future sugar beet crops is used as collateral.
The credit which TSF extends to the farmer is obtained under short term
procedures from TCZB at an interest rate of 14.4% and is on-let to the farmers
at the same rate. In 1973/74 fattening season a 6.4% Government subsidy was
applied to these funds making the net rate to the farmer 8.0% but this subsidy
was removed for subsequent fattening periods. TSF is therefore the sole
borrower from TCZB and is liable for collection and repayment of all funds
from the subborrowers.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
7.        Requirements:  The contract between TSF and the fattening farmers
requires that the farmer must submit to the guidance and supervision of TSF
technical staff. Also, eligible farmers must have access to barns acceptable
to TSF. They have to organize the purchase of cattle, negotiate a price and
arrange for their transport. Before an animal is accepted into the program
it is weighed and given a strict veterinary examination by TSF technical
representatives. These latter staff also regularly supervise the cattle
throughout the fattening period to ensure that the recommended ration is fed
and that all veterinary demands are met.
8.        The farmers are under a contractual obligation to sell the fattened
animals to the Meat and Fisheries Organisation (MFO), 1/ Etve Balik, at prices
fixed by it after negotiations with TSF. The MFO fixes a base price per
kilogram of liveweight (weight-on-the-hoof minus 7% for fill etc), in relation
to a minimum recovery of dressed meat. The present system (1974/75) is based
on 52% dressing-out with a 2% increase in price per 1% increase in yield above
base value. An additional improvement premium is applied to animals with
dressing values of more than 54% if the age is less than 3 years. This
premium is TL 0.1/kg liveweight. Near the end of the fattening period the
farmer is advised of the date he must deliver the fattened animals to the
TSF weigh scales collecting point. There MFO weighs and officially receives
them.
9.        All animals accepted into the program are insured against death
until their delivery to MFO. The farmer is paid by TSF after deduction of
credit charges including those for veterinary expenses and insurance, within
a few weeks of the date of sale regardless of whether TSF has been reimbursed
by MFO.
10.       Results:  The present situation of the program is that there are
21 district chiefs, 36 zoo technicians and 36 veterinarians directly involved
in a project covering 4,500 farmers. Indirect assistance is also available,
for supervision purposes, from the regular TSF technical staff (para 3).
Some of the veterinarians are hired by TSF from Government sources on a
temporary basis in areas where these skills are not available through TSF.
The non-veterinary staff work on regular sugar beet duties during the
growing and harvesting season. Supervision is regular (1-3 visits/week) and
thorough. The provision of feedstuff and cattle purchasing credit has allowed
animals of approximately 150 kg liveweight to be intensively fattened at net
1/   MFO was established by Government and currently operates 11 slaughter
processing plants known as "combines" with another 2 scheduled to be
opened in 1975. This present capacity restricts MFO to approximately
10% of domestic beef production. In areas where farmers, not in the
program, have experienced difficulty in having animals slaughtered
timely enough, the problem appears to be one of cold storage rather
than of slaughtering facilities. Also MFO at present only operates one
8-hour shift per day.



ANNEX 5
Page 4
rates of 0.7 kg/day. Farmers in the program experienced few delays in the
marketing of their cattle, however their counterparts outside the program often
have to retain animals more than four weeks past the scheduled date of
slaughter due to inability of MFO to process the animals. For the 1974-75
season, the MFO base price (calculated on 52% dressing out value) per kilogram
of liveweight was between TL 13.90 - 14.70 depending on the region and the
month of slaughter. However the average price received for all cattle fat-
tened in the first cycle (September-December) of the TSF program was TL 15.7/
kg due to the age premia and the high dressing percentages (approximately 56-
58% on average compared to values for animals not in the program, but pro-
cessed by MFO, of approximately 50-52%). MFO sells 70% of its production to
state institutions, notably the Army, hospitals and canteens. The weakness
of the arrangement is that Government frequently requires MFO to sell the
meat at below cost, subsidising the loss by direct payments to MFO. The
resultant effect has been a delay in payment by the state institutions and
by the Government of the subsidy. This in turn delays repayment to the
farmer or, in the case of the fattening programs, to TSF. This year (1974/75)
a regularly scheduled second fattening cycle (January to April) was inoperable
due to the failure of MFO to pay TSF which in turn could not generate suffi-
cient funds to instigate a second feeding cycle. Although these arrears have
been removed, the detrimental effect of this lag in payment for cattle bought
by MFO is of continued concern. However responsibility for resolving the
problem in the longer term has been assumed by the Chairman of the Project
Coordinating Committee together with the Ministry of Finance.
11.       The number of cattle fattened in recent years in two cycles has
peaked around 131,000 (Table 1) with first cycle numbers being approximately
twice as great as second cycle. This is due to (a) the reduced number of
animals available for fattening at the beginning of the second cycle in
January and (b) the inability to receive adequate credit to finance a second
cycle. The latter point has resulted in a failure in 1974/75 to finance a
second cycle for reasons noted in para 10.
12.       Conclusion.  The TSF program has proven to be effective in increasing
the production of meat by improving the process of fattening. The returns to
the farmer as outlined in the model (Table 2) have resulted in a very profitable
operation which uses a by-product, namely pulp + molasses, and close technical
supervision to produce a higher yield of meat from a given animal than is
obtained under traditional beef meat production systems in Turkey. The system
is particularly important in the eastern part of Turkey where livestock pro-
vide a major part of the farmers income.
13.       When questioned about the merits of the program, farmers noted the
following as their major advantages received:
-    The availability of short-term credit to purchase the
feeder stock and feedstuffs compared to the relatively
strict requirements set by other lending institutions and
the higher interest rates of private lenders



ANNEX 5
Page 5
Regularity and confidence in the marketing arrangements
which avoid extended delays before cattle are accepted for
slaughter
The insurance policy against animal loss during the fattening
period
-    Confidence in the TSF technical staff built up by in-the-field
experience, and association with a respected organisation
which provides similar technical guidance on sugar beet produc-
tion in the summer months
The principal positive aspects of this program, therefore, are the organiza-
tional strength and geographic network of TSF in association with the linkage
of credit to technical guidance, supervision and marketing arrangements.
