APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT AND AGROINDUSTRIES PROJECT
TURKEY
Ferry Boat
Background
1.        The Fruit and Vegetable Export Project Loan/Credit 762/257 TU in-
cluded a ferryboat component for which financing of 100% of the foreign ex-
change costs would be provided for purchasing two new ferryboats. The sub-
borrower would use them to establish twice weekly service from the Tuarkish
port of Izmir to Trieste (Italy) and/or Koper (Yugoslavia) principally to
carry refrigerated over-the-road trailers with fruit, vegetables, fish and
meat to European markets. In addition, general cargo would be carried on
return trips and when perishable cargo was not available for outgoing voyages.
Each ferryboat would have a dead weight tonnage of about 3,000 and be capable
of carrying 40-foot refrigerated trailers (about 65) at a minimum speed of
18.5 knots. The ferryboats would be procured by ICB, but they would be
ordered only after the Bank and the Association had approved the subborrower
and its management and overseas arrangements.
;2.       Until 1974, no potential subborrower had shown an active interest in
the ferryboat subloans. However, with increasing exports of fresh fruits and
vegetables, difficulties with the overland route to Europe and a general
increase in ocean cargo transportation in the Mediterranean, Turkish Cargo
Lines (TCL), a state economic enterprise, indicated an interest in the ferry-
boat project, to the Agricultural Bank of Turkey and the Project Unit (PU),
Fruit and Vegetable Export Project. The PU assisted by TCL prepared a feas-
ibility study which was submitted to the Bank and the Association as the
basis for requesting approval for financing the ferryboat project. The
study indicated that new ferryboat costs had increased from US$4.2 million,
each estimated at the time of appraisal to about US$11 million by June 1975.
As a result of the Bank's review and a request for more information, the PU
prepared a supplementary report which indicated that TCL would be the sub-
borrower and that used or new ferryboats under construction on contract
(resale) would be acceptable as an alternative to purchasing new ferryboats.
Investment costs would be about 25% less if such boats were available on the
world market.
3.        The concept changed from ferryboats carrying only over-the-road
trailers to the new Roll-on/Roll-off one which has become successful through-
out the world. RO/RO ferryboats carry both over-the-road trailers and
el



ANNEX 6
Page 2
special ship trailers 1/ providing considerable flexibility on shorter
shipping routes. It can operate on (a) terminal to terminal basis with small
lots of cargo carried on pallets, (b) short time, in port, (c) carrying
conventional containers of ship's trailers, and (d) lifting conventional
trucks and semitrailers which are not normal marine equipment.
4.        TCL operates 58 ships for liner, tramp and time-charter services
with a total capacity of 840,000 DWT. The Ministry of Finance accounts for
68% of TCL's paid-in capital (TL 750 million or US$ 53 million) and is TCL's
major shareholder. The remaining shares are owned by the Turkish Pension
Fund and the Turkish Maritime Bank. As of December 31, 1974 TCL's total
assets amounted to TL 3.4 billion (US$242 million).
Transport Demand
5.        In 1973, Turkish exports of fresh fruit and vegetables totaled
about 163,000 tons of which 51% went to Western Europe, 48% to Eastern Europe
and 1% to Arab countries. About 33% of the exports to Europe passed through
the ports of Izmir and Mersin. During the same year, waterborne exports of
general cargo to Europe totaled about 1.9 million tons, 60% of which went
through Adriatic ports. About 41% of the general cargo was exported from
Izmir and Mersin. Imports of waterborne general cargo, during 1973, from
Europe to Turkey was about 2.7 million tons of which 50% passed through
Adriatic ports. About 32% was imported through Mersin and Izmir. Of the
total waterborne Adriatic cargo, about 7% was carried by Turkish flag vessels,
in 1974.
6.        Fruit and vegetable exports are projected to increase to about
336,000 tons by 1980 of which 50% would be exported to Adriatic ports from
Mersin and Izmir. During the same period, general cargo has been estimated
to grow to about 3 million tons. Incremental increase of fruit and vegetable
exports from the two Turkish ports (115,000 tons) could go to the RO/RO fleet
which would have an annual outbound capacity of about 131,000 tons. However,
the increase in volume would not be fully achieved by RO/RO service because of
the seasonality of cargo flow. It is estimated that about 75,000 tons of
perishable products could be lifted with the balance of the capacity being
allocated to general cargo. The mix of general and perishable cargo would
vary over the year, but non-seasonal shippers would not accept the incon-
venience of seasonal rerouting of traffic to satisfy the demand for
pershiable cargo space.
1/   Special ship trailers with 18 ton cargo capacity are loaded at the port,
pulled onto the boat with a special tractor, pulled off at destination
with a similar tractor, and the cargo unloaded onto railcars and/or over-
the-road trailers.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
7.        Another aspect of cargo potential is the over-the-road traffic
which enters and leaves Turkey and the in-transit traffic through Turkey.
The total number of one-way trips of 40-foot trailers to, from and through
Turkey increased from 9,100 in 1968 to 25,000 in 1974, of which about 50%
were in-transit primarily destined for Iran. The total trips are projected
to increase to about 36,000 by 1980.
8.        To meet the requirements of a viable RO/RO service, TCL would need
to carry about 12% of the cargo that moved between Turkey and Adriatic ports
in 1974 as compared with the 7% it lifted by conventional shipping. Consi-
dering the improved service offered by RO/RO vessels, in terms of service
frequency, transit speed, flexibility of cargo handling and cargoes to be
handled, it would be expected that additional cargo would be attracted.
9.        In shipping, a number of operators offer comparable services but
with costs that may differ widely. To reduce the potential effects of "cut
throat" competition, carriers usually form what are referred to as "confe-
rences", although other names may be used. These are forms of agreement be-
tween the competing carriers to offer rates and conditions of transport that
are the same between one another, thus providing a form of rate stability.
Prior to 1974, the Adriatic-Turkey carriers operated on the basis of a
"Gentleman's Agreement" to follow the practices of the AMETILE conference that
covered the West Coast of Italy - Levant services. In 1974, a more formal
agreement was entered into by all but TCL to cover the Adriatic - Turkey trades
known as ALFA (Adriatic-Levant Freight Agreement). TCL has applied for
membership and it is expected that the application will be approved. At
this time, ALFA is geared only to conventional break bulk cargo due to the
general use of coaster-type ships in the trade. Adriatica, a RO/RO operator
in Adriatic region, follows ALFA for all but full trailer loads where a
"freight ALL kinds" type of rate is used. The formation of the ALFA con-
ference is viewed positively by the trade in that the group has some strength
and will persist in the long run, thus, securing a stable rate position.
Analysis and Recommendations
10.       Evaluation of the feasibility studies and updating information on
the used boat approach indicate that the RO/RO ferryboat component is tech-
nically feasible and financially viable and would provide Turkey with an
alternative shipping route to European markets, particularly for perishable
products less dependent upon the uncertainties of overland road transport
which is subject to numerous border tie-ups.
11.       Various alternative investment schedules have been explored such as
(a) buy one now and the second one when the Credit Project is effective, (b)
buy one and lease the second one as a "bare" boat with a provision to pur-
chase when funds become available and finally, (c) purchase both boats at
same time. After reviewing the alternatives, it appears best if both boats
were to be procured at the same time. The advantages include:



ANNEX 6
Page 4
(a) Provides an opportunity to purchase sister ships which would
not only reduce spare part inventory requirements but also
contribute to operating effectiveness.
(b) Permits provision of the regular and frequent service between
Turkish and Northern Adriatic ports essential to regular flow
of produce to European markets and to generating demand for
service.
(c) Encourages development of adequate port services including
new port management necessary to support a financially viable
RO/RO service.
(d) Minimizes additional procurement costs due to investment cost
increases, which might result from further delays.
12.       The physical characteristics of the RO/RO ferryboats to be procured
are:

13.       TCL is organized bike most shipping companies with a vast\net of
agents established outside the home country to solicit cargo in both ports
and major industrial centers of the world. These agents within Europe and
the United Kingdom would solicit eastbound semi-trailers and flat loads of
general cargo.
14.       Under the Assistant General Manager (Technical) is the Operations
Department which is responsible for da  to day operations of vessels in the
fleet including crewing, maintenance, storing and other activities that
relate strictly to the vessel and not to shore based services. The Assistant
General Manager for Trade would, through the Traffic Department, be responsible
for soliciting and booking cargo for the Adriatic service. Also, the group
for Conference Matters and Agreements are part of this department's functions.
Included are stevedore and port agreements that must be entered into with
respect to the new and different arrangements required of a RO/RO operation.
The navigation department is responsible for scheduling of vessels in the
fleet which would be done with the cooperation of the Adriatic Service Divi-
sion of the Traffic Department.



ANNEX 6
Page 5
15.       Lacking in the organization is any clear delineation of responsi-
bility for the establishment of new operational procedures for the cargo
handling services including the most important, the terminal operations,
which would be a vital part of the successful implementation of a RO/RO
ferryboat service. Complicating this issue is the fact that the responsi-
bility for many of the terminal stevedoring and control functions is not in
the hands of TCL but under the control of the cognizant port authorities.
Since no clear authority exists in this area, the overall Turkish port
organization is in the process of c,ange.  Due to the lack of experience in
organizing and operating RO/RO serve, technical assistance would be pro-
vided under the project to the ports by TCL for training operations personnel
the practices necessary to assure efficient cargo handling and rapid vessel
turnaround. The services of a terminal and equipment coordinator who would
be responsible for all training and supervision of the port cargo would be
provided under the project.
16.       Since sufficient funds are not available from the Loan/Credit 762/
257 TU, to finance the two RO/RO ferryboats and ancillary equipment necessary
to establish the RO/RO service, one ferryboat would be provided from L/C
762/257 TU and provisions for financing the second boat would be included
in this project. Government and TCL have indicated their willingness to
make a contribution of US$3.1 million and US$0.8 million respectively to the
investment costs.  US$6.85 million would be provided from L/C 762/257 TU and
the balance US$6.85 million would be included under this project. The pro-
posed investment program envisaged is:

18.       Retroactive financing is proposed to facilitate simultaneous

19.       The revenue, investment and operating costs used in the financial
model for the two RD/RO ferryboats are based on May 1975 prices. The as-
sumptions used for load factor and cargo rates were conservative and the
operating costs were generous. The financial rate of return is adequate and
the sensitivity analysis indicates the financial viability is not too
sensitive to reasonable changes in investment costs, operating costs and
revenue.

Economic Evaluation of Tender Offers
1.        The procedure for the economic evaluation is to emphasize the quan-
tification of the technical elements so as to better define the differences
between the various offers. An internal rate of return or Discounted Cash
Flow (DCF) will be calculated for each of the various offers considered.
2.        The first screening of the offers will be by a pure technical
delivery and price evaluation which is likely to reduce the number of offers
subject to a more thorough economic analysis to a reasonable number. The
reason for rejection of any offers must be made known to the Bank.
3.        Each of the offered vessels will be examined on a typical voyage
itinerary with a base cargo load of some 65% of a maximum capacity of 55 semi-
trailer equivalents (STE). For ships larger than 55 STE the percent utiliza-
tion is to be reduced while for ships smaller than 55 STE the utilization is
to be increased. Unless a better value can be developed by analysis of traf-
fic data it is suggested that a 1.25% change in utilization will occur for
each 100 tons of deadweight capacity or equivalent trailer capacity.
4.        Start up deadweight utilization will be applied to the base utiliza-
tion values as follows:
Start-Up             Base
util.              Util.
1st year                          80%       of       65%.
2nd year                          90%       of       65%
3rd year                          95%       of       65%
4th year and over                 100%      of        65%
5.        The operating life of the ship in Turkish Cargo Lines (TCL) serv-
ice will be 20 years less the ship's age at time of delivery.  Salvage value
may be taken at about $80 per light ship ton, but of course is neglioible in
a discounted cash flow approach.
6.        It would be expected that a baseline operating cost profile as
shown in Exhibit 2.5 would be established with appropriate annual variations
to reflect the year to year changes, particularly as may be due to special
surveys, etc. These base line costs would represent the best thinking of TCL
as to the operating costs that they would expect to see in a vessel as they
would have built it. The economic evaluation must consider the variations
from the base line cost that are reflected by the characteristics, capacities
and technical features of each offered ship.

7.        With respect to costs TCL should define the characteristics and
outfit of each ship offered to examine differences between them. In the case
of secondhand or resale ships, the technical/economic differences between
the vessels, regardless of deadweight are:
1.   Incremental costs to TCL to bring vessel up to the standard
in terms of improvements, modifications, and certain repairs.
2.   Estimated costs to be incurred over the life cycle of the
ship due to the nature of the machinery, structure or outfit
of the ship.
3.   Increased operating costs due to certain unfavorable operating
or maintenance conditions on board and vice versa.
8.        The experience of TCL should be used in evaluating the economic
effects of the technical differences on the operation of each vessel. For
example, if it is known that the one type of diesel offered may require an
expensive rebuilding each three years, then an estimate of this must be in
the cash flow at the appropriate point of time. If an offer is suitable
except for some features, such as a height of automobile deck, then the cost
of its removal can be included as part of the capital cost of the system at
time zero. Certain engines are known in general to require more maintenance



ANNEX 6
Enclosure 2
Page 3
than others or could conceivably require an additional day-work crewman which
would be a recurring cost each year above the base line standard level.
9.        With resale ships it is possible that TCL will be aware of change
orders to be made prior to delivery. The cost to perform these changes must
be included in the capital base of the ship.
10.       Finally, it should be possible to perform an incremental analvsis
of the two best systems, which permit their comparison so as to determine
the better of two competing systems, which may individually appear quite good.
11.       The following theoretical example of the data is shown:
Net Cash Flows   -   $ Thousand
Year                                Project A  Project B        A - B
0                                   (1000)      (750)         (250)
1                                    525         465            60
2                                    425         345            80
3                                    325         225           100
4                                     225        105           120
DCF Return                    21.8%       25.1%         14%
12.       In the above Project A, although having a higher investment and a
lower DCF return by itself, is superior to Project B, if the incremental
return on the additional investment is higher than the minimal acceptable
return. However, should the minimal return be greater than 14.6% then
Project B is the clear choice.

APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT AND AGROINDUSTRIES PROJECT
TURKEY
Supporting Services and Training Programs
A. Background
1.        Two Government agencies would be involved in extending technical
assistance to agroindustry subborrowers: the Project Unit (PU) of the Ministry
of Agriculture and TCZB. A single agency, the Supervised Credit Division of
TCZB would implement the Supervised Credit Program (SCP), and another agency
the Turkiye Seker Fabrikalari (TSF) would carry out the beef fattening com-
ponent. Finally, the Turkish Cargo Lines (TCL) would carry out the ferry
boat operations. Aside from the specific responsibilities for implementing
project components which TCZB shares with the respective project agencies,
TCZB as Turkey's major agricultural credit institution would also be respons-
ible for subloan evaluation, based on financial analysis, loan appraisal and
administration.
B.  Project Coordination
2.        Coordination between the implementing agencies would be carried out
by a Project Coordinating Committee. The terms of reference for the Project
Coordinator are given below:
(a) TCZB staff member with university degree in business admin-
istration, economics, agricultural economics or other rele-
vant discipline; 10 years experience in development financ-
ing of which 5 in managerial position.
(b) While maintaining his TCZB position, his functions in the
Committee would be coordinating day-to-day implementation
of the projects between PU, TSF and TCZB. He would
raise all matters requiring high level interagency resolu-
tion to the Project Coordinating Committee through the
Secretary of the Committee.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
C.  Needs for and Scope of Technical Assistance
3.        Four areas have been identified in which technical assistance would
be required. The following table gives a summary of the main features of
the proposed technical assistance program.

/1   The UNDP Resident Representative in Ankara has indicated availability of
UNDP funds for two projects for the training project staff. Under the
proposals now being formulated, UNDP/FAO and UNDP/UNIDO experts would
carry out the tasks listed in (b) and (c), respectively. Because these
UNDP projects have not finalized, Bank financing of these items is pro-
visionally provided under the project. If such projects are eventually
approved the designated Bank funds would be cancelled.



ANNEX 7
Page 3
D. Assistance to TCZB Towards Institutional Improvement
4.        TCZB would obtain the services of consultants to assist in under-
taking studies of key problem areas, with a view to institutional improve-
ments. The consultants would also help in implementing the recommendations
emanating from the studies. Because of the variety of skills needed, it will
be necessary to employ a consulting firm which can draw on professional ex-
pertise in organization, management accounting, financial planning and devel-
opment lending.
5.        The assistance program is expected to be carried out in two phases:
(a)  Analysis and recommendations:
i.   6 man-months of a bank management specialist
ii.   6 man-months of a credit specialist
iii. 6-man-mouths of an accounting specialist
(b) Implementation:
1.   30 man-months of a system analysis specialist
ii.   30 man-months of an accounting specialist
6.        Following discussions, the Turkish authorities have indicated that
the following subjects would be included in the abovementioned studies:
I.   Practices and procedures of lending operations, including
project appraisal and implementation:
(a) application of latest methods of economic and financial
evaluation to the appraisal of agroindustries projects;
(b) criteria and methods for updating economic and financial
evaluation of projects during period of project implemen-
tation until start-up of project operations;
(c) methods of evaluation to be applied in appraisal of super-
vised agricultural credit projects;
(d) alternative methods of analysis on more comprehensive
basis of economic and financial viability of projects for
which investment loans are made by TCZB, including aspects
of income generation and cash flow;



ANNEX 7
Page 4
(e) alternative types of loan security; and
(f) loan supervision and collection procedures.
ii.   Organization and management:
(a)  prixciples for the general administration of TCZB;
(b)  assignment and coordination of functions among departments
of TCZB; and
(c) management principles for administration within departments.
iii.   Accounting plans, principles, systems and procedures as effective
instruments of management:
(a) financial analysis of TCZB's annual balance sheets and profit
and loss accounts to determine TCZB's creditworthiness;
(b) techniques and procedures for portfolio evaluation;
(c) further development of data bank created by TCZB's Method
and Research Department, including methods of data collection
and retrieval; and
(d) methods of classification, analysis and presentation of data,
including use of operations research, as tool for decision-
making by TCZB's managers.
iv.   Operational and financial management and planning, including long-
term planning and control of pperations, and long-term projections
of financial requirements and resources.
v.   Research methods and procedures.
E. Program for SCP
Objectives
7.        The training program aims at upgrading the competence of SCP staff
of TCZB as required for effective implementation of this component. While
staff performance has been remarkably good during the formative stages of these
cre4it activities, there has been substantial turnover especially among the
agricultural engineers (Annex 3). Under the project, SCP activities would be
expanded and broadened to cover new provinces and to include more target group
farmers among the beneficiaries. The training program is essential to meet
the challenge of this new dimension.



ANNEX 7
Page 5
Areas of Emphasis
8.        The training would concentrate on:
-    farm management, including relevant technical knowledge;
-    public relations and agricultural extensioni and communication
techniques;
-    loan appraisal techniques based on farm plans, farm budgets
and cash flow analysis;
-    monitoring and evaluating the impact of SCP loans.
Scope
9.        The training would be pragmatic.  Although including courses and/or
fellowships for management level staff (super engineers and branch managers)
it would be oriented to the needs of SCP field staff, including the village
credit agents required for implementing the SCP short term credit program.
It would concentrate on:
-    Pre-service training for new staff;
-    in-service training for experienced and new staff;
-    training fellowships for selected management, supervisory
and training staff.
Technical Assistance Requirements
10.       Technical and financial assistance from UNDP/FAO, as tentatively
agreed with the UN Resident Representative, would include the following expert
services:
Man-Months
Farm credit                                             30
Farm management                                         24
Short-term consultants as needed                        18
Total for FAO as executing agency                       72
Further details on this technical assistance component are in Annex 3.



ANNEX 7
Page 6
Terms of Reference
Farm Credit Expert
11.  (a)  University degree in agricultural agroeconomics, agriculture or
agricultural engineering; 10 years professional experience in
preparation, evaluation and supervison of agricultural credit
projects, a considerable part of which should have been acquired
in developina countries.
(b) Assist TCZB's SCP Division in preparing a training program for its
supervisory staff, including SCP technicians and agricultural
engineers, Branch managers and Headquarters staff responsible for
SCP activities as well as village credit agents to be selected
for implementing SCP's short-term credit program. Provide formal
and in-service training to SCP supervisory staff in preparation,
appraisal and supervision of SCP supported projects as well as
in monitoring and evaluating the impact of SCP loans.
(c) 30 man-months.
Farm Management Expert
12.  (a)  University or higher technical institute degree in agricultural
engineering or agriculture; 10 years' professional experience
in farm management and agricultural extension, a considerable
part of which should have been acquired in developing countries.
(b) Provide formal and, especially, in-service training in farm
management, including subject matter knowledge, and agricultural
extension techniques to TCZB's SCP supervisory field staff (agri-
cultural technicians and engineers) as well as to village credit
agents.  Assist TCZB in assembling and analyzing relevant farm
management data, in preparing farm plans and cash flow projec-
tions as a basis for farm loan appraisal. Together with the
credit expert and TCZB staff, prepare manuals for staff guidance
in farm loan appraisal and supervision.
F. Training Program Agroindustries for PU
Objectives
13.       The program would aim at upgrading and strengthening the capabilities
of PU and TCZB staff to assist subborrowers in preparing applications eligible
for TCZB subloans.



ANNEX 7
Page 7
Areas of Emphasis
14.       The program would concentrate on the following topics:
-    provision of raw materials including arrangements with producers
and marketing firms to ensure full utilization of installed
capacity;
-    analysis of domestic and export market prospects for agro-
industry products and by-products;
-    engineering analysis, including plant location, process
selection, plant design, preparation of tender documents,
technical specifications and bid evaluation:
-    economic and financial analysis of subprojects, including cash
flow projections, financial planning of fixed investment and
working capital requirements and rate of return calculations;
-    business management techniques, including marketing, cost
accounting, inventory control and quality control;
-    monitoring and evaluating the impact of agroindustry investments.
Scope
15.       The program would have a practical orientation and would include:
-    pre-service training for new staff;
-    in-service training;
-    training fellowships.
Technical Assistance Requirements
16.       Technical and financial assistance from UNDP/UNIDO, as tentatively
agreed with the UN Resident Representative, would include the following ex-
pert services:
Man/months
Training Program for PU
Business management and marketing                 36
Engineering                                       24
Economic and financial analysis and
planning                                        24
Further details on this technical assistance component are in Annex 4.

Terms of Reference
Industrial Engineer
17.  (a)  University degree in process engineering; 10 years' professional
experience in food processing and agroindustries, a considerable
part of which should have been acquired in developing countries.
(b)  Assist PU in preparing feasibility studies and subloan applications
with a view to all engineering aspects including the provision
of raw materials, location of plants. process selection, plant
design; train PU staff in these matters and prepare them, in
turn, to help entrepreneurs and cooperatives prepare technically
viable agroindustry projects.
(c) 24 man-months.
Financial Analyst for PU
18.       (a)  University degree in business administration or economics; 10
years' professional experience in financial and economic analysis
and planning of industrial projects, part of which should have
been acquired in developing countries.
(b)  Assist PU in economic and financial analysis of subborrowers'
projects; train PU staff in economic and financial analysis
and planning, applying modern methods such as cash flow
projections, internal rate of return calculations, and
sensitivity analyses.
(c) 24 months.
Business Management and Marketing Expert
19.       (a)  University degree in business administration or economics; 10
years' professional experience in business management in agro-
industrial enterprises including marketing of agroindustrial
products, a considerable part of which should have been ac-
quired in developing countries.
(b) Assist PU in evaluating the marketing aspects of feasibility
stuidies and subloan applications; compile and keep up-to-date
relevant statistical information to enable PU to advise
entrepreneurs of market prospects and marketing techniques
and assist PU in advising subborrowers (including cooperatives)
on other matters of business administration as a supporting
measure to loan supervision, especially providing consulting



ANNEX 7
Page 9
assistance to subborrowers in accounting, cost accounting,
inventory control and organization. Train PU staff in the
fields mentioned above.
(c) 36 months.

AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT AND AGROINDUSTRIES PROJECT
TURKEY
Economic Rate of Return Calculations
1.        The economic rate of return for each project component and for the
whole project were calculated by aggregating the representative models de-
scribed in Annexes 3, 4, 5 and 6, and adding the costs of supporting services.
2.        In the Supervised Credit component, the models 1-8 (Annex 3, Tables
6-11) cover 70% of the total investments for integrated farm development. Sim-
ilarly, the agroindustries models cover 49% of the contemplated investment costs.
It is assumed that the remaining investments would have similar benefit-cost-
ratios as the investments covered by the models. To arrive at the full amount
for each component, therefore, the sums of all input and output values are
divided by 0.70 and 0.49, respectively.
3.        In calculating the rates of return, world market prices were applied
where appropriate. Labor was shadow priced according to the marginal output
foregone.
4.        To convert local costs and benefits into foreign exchange equivalents,
various conversion factors have been applied. The corresponding shadow ex-
change rates vary between TL 16.7 to 17.3/US$ as compared to the official ex-
change rate of TL 14/US$.
5.        For the agroindustry plants, it is assumed that there is approximately
a six-month lag between incurring operating costs and the derivation of benefits
resulting from them. In the financial analysis, this has been taken into account
by including interest on seasonal credit in the operating costs. To reflect
adequately this six-month lag in the economic analysis, two calculations have
been made: one with a non-lagged benefit stream and another with a benefit
stream lagged by one year. The rate of return for the agroindustry component
is then the average of computations with lagged and non-lagged benefit streams.
6.        Further information regarding assumptions for the benefit and cost
streams of each project component is given in Tables 1-4.
7.        The results are presented below:

Project Components

Credit    Fattening  Industries    boat   Project

Economic Rate of Return         52       Very high/l      19          33      36
Sensitivity/2:  Cost + 10%      36       Very high/i      16          25      30
Benefits-10%   35       Very high/i      16          25      29
/1   There is a 100-120 days lag between the purchase date of the feeder and
the sales date of the fattened cattle.  Computed on a yearly basis, the
net benefit stream is positive each year, so that the rate of return is
not defined. If computed on a 4-months basis, no unique rate of return
can be found. However, the statement "very high" can be verified by
computing the rate of return for a one-year operation only, which would
be over 60%.
/2   Since the agroindustries sector is in a rather strong bargaining position
vis-a-vis agriculture, the conventional sensitivity analysis appears too
pessimistic. It is more likely that a reduction of sales will result in
a similar decrease in the purchase prices of raw materials. Therefore,
when benefits are reduced by 10%, it is assumed that the raw material
costs are reduced by the same percentage. In any case, raw materials
have been excluded from the cost sensitivity test and included with
benefits in the benefit sensitivity test.
